ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-item {
    counter-increment: list-counter-alpha;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-alpha, lower-alpha);
}

/* Level 3: Lowercase roman numerals */

    counter-reset: list-counter-roman;
}

    counter-increment: list-counter-roman;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-roman, lower-roman);
}

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="eps-110-pdf-notes-am-student-pursuing">EPS 110 pdf Notes - Am student pursuing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#eps-110-pdf-notes-am-student-pursuing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Introduction to psychology (Kenyatta University)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-c5140b9b.jpeg" alt="img-0.jpeg"></p>
<p>Scan to open on Studocu</p>
<p>Studocu is not sponsored or endorsed by any college or university</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ku-eps-100-notes">KU EPS 100 NOTES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ku-eps-100-notes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-psychology">DEFINITION OF PSYCHOLOGY.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-psychology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The word psychology comes from the Greek words <em>psyche</em> which means mind/soul/spirit and <em>logos</em> meaning knowledge or study.</p>
<p>Formally defined, psychology is the <em>scientific study of behavior and mental processes of humans and other animals</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="psychology-as-a-science">Psychology as a science.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychology-as-a-science" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Psychology is a scientific study.</p>
<p>Psychological research uses <em>scientific methods</em> to provide enlightening and reliable information about behavior. Science is derived from the Latin word scire i.e. to know. Science avoids mere opinions, intuitions and guesses and strives to know by using <em>objective evidence</em>. Science uses <em>logic</em> to reason about the possible causes of a phenomenon and then <em>tests</em> the resulting ideas psychology relies on <em>empirical evidence</em> i.e. information gained from direct observation. The information is <em>collected systematically</em> through carefully designed experiments; various assumptions can be validated or refuted.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-study-of-behavior">The study of behavior.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-study-of-behavior" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Behavior means the <em>outward (overt)</em> observable acts of a person/ animal, either alone or in a group.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="study-of-mental-processes">Study of mental processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#study-of-mental-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Psychologists also study <em>covert</em> behaviors. These are <em>private, internal activities</em>, such as <em>thinking; dreaming; remembering; perceiving; reasoning; problem solving</em> and other mental events.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="study-of-humans-and-other-animals">Study of humans and other animals<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#study-of-humans-and-other-animals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Psychology includes the study of human as well as animal behavior. Psychologists study rats, dogs, cats, insects, pigeons, monkeys, chimpanzees, turtles and other animals.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-goals-of-psychology">THE GOALS OF PSYCHOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-goals-of-psychology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i) <strong>Describe behavior</strong>: Answering psychological questions requires a careful description of behavior. This is based on making a detailed record of behavioral observation.</p>
<p>ii) <strong>Understanding behavior</strong>: Explaining an eventStating the causes of behavior and determining how they led to the described behavior.</p>
<ul>
<li>E.g. research on bystander apathy reveals that people often fail to help when other possible helpers are nearby. Why? Because a diffusion of responsibility occurs. Basically, no one feels personally obligated to assist. As a result, the more potential helpers there are, the less likely it is that anyone will help (Darley 2000; Darley and Latane, 1968). This can help us explain a perplexing problem.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>1</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>iii) Predicting behavior: This is the ability to forecast/ foretell behavior accurately. e.g.</p>
<ul>
<li>How people learn most effectively.</li>
<li>Bystander apathy  makes a prediction of the chances of getting help  (having many potential helpers is no guarantee that anyone will help).</li>
</ul>
<p>iv) Controlling or influencing phenomena: This refers to altering conditions that affect behavior e.g. if you suggest changes in a classroom that help students learn better, you have exerted control. If you help a student overcome terrible exam/ test anxiety, control is involved.</p>
<p>N.B. Psychological control must be used wisely and humanely.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="major-perspectives-followed-in-the-study-of-psychology">MAJOR PERSPECTIVES FOLLOWED IN THE STUDY OF PSYCHOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#major-perspectives-followed-in-the-study-of-psychology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A perspective is a broad look at a topic/ issue.</p>
<p>A number of perspectives have been used to explain human thought and behaviour.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-the-psychoanalytic-perspective">i) The Psychoanalytic perspective.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-the-psychoanalytic-perspective" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The psychodynamic perspective originated with the work of <em>Sigmund Freud</em>. This view of psychology and human behavior emphasizes the role of the unconscious mind, early childhood experiences, and interpersonal relationships to explain human behavior and to treat people suffering from mental illnesses. He viewed psychological traits and disorders as having an origin in sexual and aggressive drives</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-the-behavioural-perspective-early-20th-century">ii) The Behavioural perspective (early 20th century)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-the-behavioural-perspective-early-20th-century" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Behaviour theorists include B.F. Skinner and J,B. Watson.</li>
<li>Proposes that behaviour is a product of learning</li>
<li>It strove to make psychology a more scientific discipline by focusing purely on observable behavior.</li>
<li>Today, the behavioral perspective is still concerned with how behaviors are learned and reinforced.</li>
<li>Behavioral principles are often applied in mental health settings, where therapists and counselors use these techniques to explain and treat a variety of illnesses</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-the-biological-perspective">iii) The biological perspective<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-the-biological-perspective" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This helps us understand how the body and the brain work in unison to create emotions, memories and sensory experiences.</li>
<li>This perspective studies evolutionary and hereditary influences on behaviour, how messages are transmitted within the body, and how blood chemistry is related to moods and motives.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iv-the-humanistic-perspective">iv) The humanistic perspective<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-the-humanistic-perspective" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This school of thought known as humanistic psychology emerged during the 1950s.</li>
<li>This perspective emphasizes the role of motivation on thought and behavior.</li>
<li>It emphasizes that the human being has the capacity to deliberately choose a meaningful life pattern and therefore grow to greater maturity and fulfillment.</li>
<li>It was influenced greatly by the work of prominent humanists such as Carl Rogers and Abraham Maslow.</li>
<li>Concepts such as self-actualization are an essential part of this perspective.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>v) Cognitive Perspective</p>
<ul>
<li>This perspective began during the 1960s.</li>
<li>Cognition is the ability to utilize the mind in terms of mental processes such as memory, thinking, problem solving, language and decision-making.</li>
<li>In this perspective, psychologists explore the way human beings use their mental processes to reason and solve problems.</li>
<li>Influenced by psychologists such as Jean Piaget and Albert Bandura, this perspective has grown tremendously in recent decades.</li>
</ul>
<p>iv) Cross-cultural perspective</p>
<ul>
<li>This is a fairly new perspective in which psychologists and researchers look at human behavior across different cultures.</li>
<li>It looks at cultural and ethnic similarities and differences on both psychological and social functioning.</li>
<li>The assumption is that each person has a cultural and ethnic background which influences their thinking and behavior depending on the environment in which they are.</li>
</ul>
<p>GROWTH OF PSYCHOLOGY</p>
<p>NB: The roots of psychology lie in philosophy (use of logic and specialization to understand the nature of reality, experience, and values) on the one hand and physiology on the other (Study of biological workings of the body, including the brain).</p>
<p>Philosophers such as Plato, Aristotle, Descartes and Locke  raised provocative questions about human thoughts, feelings and behavior. E.g.</p>
<ul>
<li>Renes Descartes (French) focused attention on the distinction between mind and body.</li>
<li>John Locke (English philosopher) stressed that all human knowledge arises from experience of the entire world and from reflection about it.</li>
</ul>
<p>Among the early scientific psychologists were the structuralists.</p>
<p>Approaches to Psychology/schools of thought</p>
<p>A school of thought comprises a system of ideas that a proponent wishes to promote. Theories are based on schools of thought.</p>
<ol>
<li>Structuralism</li>
</ol>
<p>Wilhelm Wundt (1832  1920): Founder of scientific psychology.</p>
<ul>
<li>Set up the first psychology laboratory in 1879 in Leipzig, Germany.</li>
<li>He defined the task of psychology as the systematic study of the structure of the conscious adult mind.</li>
<li>Structuralism sought to break experiences into basic elements or structures, in the same way that a substance like water could be broken into molecules of hydrogen and oxygen.</li>
<li>He sought to identify the building blocks of consciousness i.e.</li>
</ul>
<p>i) Sensations  which arise from the eyes, ears and other sense organs.
ii) Feelings e.g. fear, anger and love</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>3</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The goal of structuralism was to describe the rules that determine how particular sensations or feelings may occur at the same time or in sequence, combining in various ways into mental structures.</p>
<ul>
<li>Wundt was primarily interested in selective attention  the process by which we determine what we are going to attend to at any given moment.</li>
<li>He borrowed a tool of philosophy i.e. introspection (looking inward) for studying mental processes.</li>
<li>One important product of the Leipzig laboratory was its students, who carried new, scientific psychology to the universities in other countries. i.e.</li>
<li>Stanley Hall  established the first American Psychology Laboratory at John Hopkins University in 1883.</li>
<li>J.M. Cattell  first American to be called a professor of psychology at the University of Pennsylvania in 1888.</li>
<li>British born Edward Bradford Titchener who went to Cornell University (USA). He broke down consciousness into 3 basic elements:</li>
<li>physical</li>
<li>feelings</li>
<li>images</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="limitations">Limitations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#limitations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>If an experience is broken into basic elements, it may not retain its essential character when subjected to this reductionist approach.</li>
<li>Introspection  often altered the nature of the conscious mental processes (analyzing what you are experience changes the experience.)</li>
<li>The observation of structuralists could not be objectively repeated with the same results (results were different.)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contributions">Contributions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contributions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The study of perception and sensation continues to be a part of contemporary psychology.</li>
<li>Looking inward (introspection) is still used in studies of hypnosis, meditation, problem-solving, moods e.t.c</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-functionalism">2. Functionalism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-functionalism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Adherents of functionalism sought to understand how our minds help us to adapt to the world around us i.e. how to function in it.</li>
<li>Functionalists were strongly influenced by Charles Darwin (1809- 1882), whose theory of evolution by natural selection stressed that some organisms possess characteristics that enable them to survive and reproduce more fruitfully than others (Survival for the fittest). Darwin called these characteristics adaptations.</li>
<li>Functionalists applied Darwins theory to mental characteristics.</li>
<li>E.g. William James (1842  1910), who set up the first psychology laboratory in the USA at Harvard University, studied the ways in which consciousness helps an individual survive and adapt to the environment.</li>
<li>He wrote a book  Principles of Psychology in 1890, which details his view of the nature of psychology.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Functionalists hold the view that psychological states or processes, such as consciousness evolved because they serve particular functions, such as guiding the activities of the individual.
&gt; They wanted to find out how various mental processes, such as perception, learning, thinking, help people to adapt and survive.
&gt; To accomplish this purpose, they continued to use introspection in their research. However, they also introduced another research method: collecting data from observation of human and animal behavior.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contributions">Contributions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contributions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Their emphasis on Darwins theory of natural selection and its links between human and non-human animals led them to theorize that human psychology is related to the psychology of animals. Observation of animals could provide clues to human behavior.</li>
<li>Focus on issues of society e.g. improving methods of education.</li>
<li>Expanded the data of psychology to include observations of behavior.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iii-evolutionary-approach">iii) Evolutionary approach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-evolutionary-approach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; Based on Charles Darwins book The Origin of Species
&gt; Darwin argued that the forms of life we see today are the result of evolution- of changes in life forms that occur over many generations.
&gt; He said that evolution occurs through natural selection, which promotes survival of the fittest individuals.
&gt; Most evolutionists today see natural selection operating at the level of genes.
&gt; Genes that result in characteristics and behaviours that are adaptive and useful in a certain environment will enable the -creatures that inherited them to survive and reproduce, thereby passing those genes on to the next generation.
&gt; Genes that result in characteristics that are not adaptive in that environment are not passed on to subsequent generations, because the creatures possessing them do not survive to produce.
&gt; Central to the evolutionary psychology approach is the idea that certain cognitive strategies and goals are so important that natural selection has built them into our brains.
&gt; It asserts that the patterns of behaviour seen in a species are products of evolution.
&gt; It therefore focuses on the evolutionary origins of behavior patterns and mental processes, exploring what adaptive value they have or had and what function they serve or served in our emergence as a distinct species.
&gt; They ask  How do human beings get to be the way they are?
&gt; They study such diverse topics as perception, language, helping others (altruism), parenting, happiness, sexual attraction and mate selection, jealousy and violence.
&gt; By studying such phenomena in different species, different habitats, different cultures, and in males and females, evolutionary psychologists seek to understand the basic programs that guide thinking and behavior.
&gt; They believe that general cognitive strategies are inborn.
&gt; They see the mind as hardwired, so that human beings are predisposed to think and act in certain ways.
&gt; They contend that these fixed programs evolved hundreds of thousands of years ago among our ancestors.
&gt; Hence many of the genes we possess today are the result of natural selection.
&gt; The human mind was sculpted by natural selection, and it is this evolved organ that constitutes the subject matter of psychology.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Their approach assumes that the behaviour of animals and humans today is due to evolution through natural selection. E.g.
&gt; Aggression- form of territory protection
&gt; Gender differences- male selection preferences
&gt; Helping one's genes survive in future generations</p>
<p>iv) Psychodynamic approach: Sigmund Freud 1856- 1939</p>
<p>&gt; Rooted in Freud's psychoanalysis
&gt; He was a Viennese physician, specializing in neurology (study of treatment of diseases of the brain and nervous system).
&gt; He proposed that the mind is made up of the id, ego, and the superego
&gt; He believed that mental life is like an iceberg  a small part is expressed to view.
&gt; He called the area of the mind that lies outside of personal awareness the unconscious.
&gt; He assumes that behaviour and mental processes reflect constant and mostly unconscious psychological struggles raging within each person.
&gt; He viewed the unconscious as a dynamic cauldron of primitive sexual and aggressive drives, forbidden desires, nameless fears and wishes and traumatic childhood memories. Anxiety, depression, or other disorders are seen as overt signs of inner turmoil. Although repressed (or hidden from awareness), unconscious impulses press on the conscious mind and find expression in disguised or altered form, including dreams, mannerisms, slips of the tongue and symptoms of mental illness as well as in socially acceptable pursuits such as art and literature
&gt; To uncover the unconscious, Freud developed the technique of free association, in which the client lies on a couch, recounts dreams and says whatever comes to mind.</p>
<p>Contributions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Freud came up with a developmental approach to an individual's personality (development proceeds through stages). It recognized the significance of early childhood experiences as affecting later behavior. (e.g. feeding, toilet training). First years of life help shape adult personality.</li>
<li>He recognized the significance of motivation</li>
<li>He recognized the importance of anxiety as an antecedent for behavioral disorders</li>
<li>Laid a foundation for the study of personality and psychological disorders</li>
<li>His notion of the unconscious, with its irrational urges and drives provided insight into understanding the emotional lives of people. It encouraged psychologists to consider the impact on behavior of processes not immediately available to conscious inspection</li>
<li>He helped to legitimize the study of human sexuality</li>
<li>Took a strong stand on determinism i.e. nothing is an accident. If we probe deeply enough, we will find the causes of every thought and action</li>
<li>Interpretations of the various forms of art and literature fall within a Freudian framework</li>
<li>Created psychoanalysis  the first talking cure</li>
</ul>
<p>Criticisms:</p>
<ul>
<li>Unverified theoretical constructs  some of his theories and hypothesis could not be put to test e.g. structure of personality psychic energy</li>
<li>Unreliable data e.g. from free association</li>
<li>Fictional concepts e.g. Oedipus complex, castration anxiety</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>He made the disconcerting suggestion that people are not masters of their own mind (behaviour is governed by unconscious forces)</li>
<li>Controversial proposal that behaviour is greatly influenced by how people cope with their sexual urges</li>
</ul>
<p>v) Behaviorism</p>
<p>Founded by John B. Watson (1878-1958).
For Watson, psychology was the study of observable measurable behavior
He stressed the stimulus  response (S-R) approach to psychology. He simply observed the relationship between stimuli (events in the environment) and an animals responses
Watson adopted Russian physiologist Ivan Pavlovs (1849-1936) concept of conditioning to explain most behavior.
&gt; Ivan Pavlov noted that the dogs in his laboratory began to salivate as soon as they had their feeder coming, even before they could see the food. He decided to find out whether salivation, an automatic reflex, could be shaped by learning. He began by repeatedly pairing the sound of a buzzer/bell with the presence of food. Next he observed what happened when the buzzer was sounded without introducing food. After repeated pairings, the dogs salivated to the buzzer alone. He called this simple form of training conditioning. The new school of psychology was inspired by observation, followed by rigorous experiment.
A conditioned response is a learned reaction to a particular stimulus. Watson eagerly claimed:
Give me a dozen healthy infants, well-formed and my own special world to bring them up in and Ill guarantee to take any one at random and train him to become any type of specialist I might select  doctor, lawyer, artist, merchant-chief, and yes beggar man and thief (Watson, 1913/1994).</p>
<p>Watson attempted to demonstrate that all psychological phenomena - even Freuds unconscious motivations - are the result of conditioning.
In one of his experiments, he attempted to create a conditioned fear response in a two month old boy-Little Albert. Albert visited Watsons laboratory and was delighted by a tame, furry white rat, but became visibly frightened when Watson banged a steel bar with a hammer just behind the infants head. On the second visit, Watson placed the rat near Albert and the moment the baby reached out and touched the rat, Watson banged the hammer. After half a dozen pairings, little Albert began crying the instant the rat was introduced, without any banging. Further experiments found that Albert was frightened by anything white and furry- rabbit, dog, coat, cotton wool e.t.c. i.e. had generalized .Watson also demonstrated that fears could be eliminated by conditioning (reconditioning).
One of the best known behaviorists B.F. Skinner (1904-1990), fervently believed that psychologists should only study observable and, measurable behavior. He added a new element to the behaviorists repertoire of reinforcement. If the consequences of behavior are reinforcing, we are likely to repeat the behavior. Conversely, if a behavior produces an undesirable outcome (punishment), we are less likely to do it again.</p>
<p>Contributions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Skinners work in operant conditioning shows the effects of reinforcement on behavior</li>
<li>Skinner has extensive writings on language learning, programmed instruction, philosophy of science and politics</li>
<li>Emphasis on objective observation</li>
<li>Behaviorists insights have improved psychotherapy and education</li>
</ul>

studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Criticisms:</p>
<ul>
<li>Many behaviorists objected to the study of mental processes
These objections have been refuted by subsequent research.</li>
</ul>
<p>vi) Gestalt psychology</p>
<p>&gt; Gestalt psychology  is taken from the German word Gestalt, which means whole or Form
&gt; It emphasized the overall pattern of thoughts or experience.
&gt; It was pioneered by a group of psychologists in Germany i.e.
&gt; - Max Wertheimer (1880-1943)
&gt; - Wolfgang Kohler (1887-1967)
&gt; - Kurt Koffka (1886-1941)
&gt; Gestalt psychologists studied thinking, learning and perception as whole units, not by analyzing experiences into parts.
&gt; Their slogan was The whole is greater than the sum of its parts e.g.
&gt; - If we look at a tree we just that, a tree, not a series of isolated leaves and branches
&gt; - A flight of birds  focus is on the whole flock, nor individual birds. The flock is the perceptual unit.
&gt; It is a mistake according to max Wertheimer, to analyze psychological events into pieces or elements as the structuralists did. Like a melody (made up of musical notes put together), many experiences cannot be broken into smaller units.</p>
<p>Contributions</p>
<ul>
<li>Studies of perception have been influenced by the Gestalt viewpoint. Perception is our gateway to the world; if our perceptions are not accurate, our corresponding thoughts and feelings will be based on a distorted view of reality.</li>
<li>The research of the Gestaltists addressed how the brain works, and today Gestaltism has become integrated into studies of the brain itself.</li>
<li>Has also paved way for the modern study of personality.</li>
</ul>
<p>vii) Humanistic psychology/Phenomenological approach</p>
<p>&gt; Proponents
&gt; - Abraham Maslow (1908  1970)
&gt; - Carl Rogers (1902  1987)
&gt; According to humanistic psychology:
&gt; - People have positive values
&gt; - Free will
&gt; - Deep inner creativity which allows them to choose life  fulfilling paths to personal growth.
&gt; It emphasizes: Love, creativity, belonging, self  esteem, self  expression, spirituality, and self  actualization (developing ones potential fully and becoming the best person possible.)
&gt; Humanists also stress more subjective factors such as:
&gt; - Self  image (perception of your body, personality and capabilities.)
&gt; - Self- evaluation (appraising yourself as good or bad.)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Frame of reference (mental perspective used to interpret events.)</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; Humanists seek to understand how we perceive ourselves and experience the world.</p>
<p>&gt; Behaviour is determined by each persons capacity to choose how to think and act.</p>
<p>&gt; These choices are driven by each individuals unique perception of the world. E.g. if you see the world as a friendly place, you are likely to be optimistic and secure. If you perceive it as hostile, you are likely to be defensive and fearful.</p>
<p>&gt; Proponents of this approach argue that behaviour and mental processes can be fully understood only by understanding the perceptions and feelings of individuals.</p>
<p>&gt; Humanistic psychologists also believe that people are essentially good, that they are in control of themselves, and that they have an innate tendency to grow toward their highest potential.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contributions">Contributions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contributions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Optimistic - views the outlook for personal fulfillment optimistically and positively</li>
<li>It puts forward the notion that man is basically good and has tremendous possibilities for self  fulfillment.</li>
<li>Has increased psychologists awareness of the importance of such things as love, feeling needed, personal fulfillment and self  esteem.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="criticisms">Criticisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#criticisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Reliance on non  scientific approach to understanding human behavior. The theories lack any empirical validity.</li>
<li>Uses introspection, which is unreliable.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="viii-the-cognitive-approach">viii) The cognitive approach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viii-the-cognitive-approach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; Emerged in the 1950s and 1960s.</p>
<p>&gt; Proponents:
&gt; - Herbert A. Simon (Computer psychologist/ scientists)
&gt; - Allan Newell.
&gt; - Noam Chomsky  linguist
&gt; - Gardener (1985)</p>
<p>&gt; It emphasizes the study of our mental processes in the broadest sense: thinking, feeling, learning, remembering, and making decisions and judgments e.t.c.</p>
<p>&gt; Focuses on how we take in, mentally represent, and store information; how we perceive and process that information, and how cognitive processes are related to our behavior.</p>
<p>&gt; Focus on the above to understand individual and social behaviours, from decision making and problem solving to interpersonal attraction and intelligence.</p>
<p>&gt; Study also the building blocks of cognition and determine how they produce complex behaviours such as remembering, writing a word or making a decision.</p>
<p>&gt; Cognitive psychologists have begun to address questions about the neurological mechanisms that underlie such cognitive processes as learning, memory, intelligence and emotion giving rise to the rapidly expanding field of cognitive neuroscience. It blends cognitive psychology and neuroscience (the study of the brain). Cognitive neuroscientists argue that the mind is what the brain does and therefore hope to discover the nature, organization and operation of mental operations by studying the brain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ix-positive-psychology">ix) Positive psychology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ix-positive-psychology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; Another emerging perspective is positive psychology.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>9</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; It focuses on positive experiences, including: subjective feelings of happiness and well being, development of such individual traits such as intimacy, integrity, leadership, altruism and wisdom, kinds of families, work settings and communities that encourage individuals to flourish, nurturance of mental wellness, and relationship between positive emotions and physical health. Emphas is on building of positive qualities</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="x-individual-differences">x) Individual Differences<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#x-individual-differences" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The individual differences school of thought became prominent after 1930s.</li>
<li>Its main proponent was Sir. Francis Galton.</li>
<li>Galton asserted that behaviour could be inherited.</li>
<li>He justified this by giving results of a study on his family tree. He was generally impressed by the great number of exceptional people in his family, for example, geniuses like Charles Darwin (his half cousin).</li>
<li>Galton is accredited with formulation of mental tests and the study of individual differences. His is the first person to introduce statistical concepts in the field of psychology, one of them being the concept of correlation coefficient. A correlation coefficient is a numerical index used to show the degree of similarity between two or more sets of data.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contemporary-psychology">CONTEMPORARY PSYCHOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contemporary-psychology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is not dominated by any single theoretical perspective.</li>
<li>Contemporary psychologists tend to see the different perspectives as complementary e.g. when they study aggression, psychologists no longer limit their explanations to the behavioral view (aggressive behavior is learned); or the Freudian perspective (aggression is an expression of unconscious hostility) but also use other theories e.g. evolutionary and cognitive psychology (Eclectic approach)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="branches-fields-specialties-of-psychology">BRANCHES/ FIELDS/SPECIALTIES OF PSYCHOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#branches-fields-specialties-of-psychology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i) Cognitive psychology: Focuses on processes such as thinking, attention, perception, decision making, reasoning, concept formation, memory, language, problem-solving, creativity and related aspects of mental processes. It is interested in one's internal representation or cognitive maps.
ii) Developmental psychology: Focuses on human growth and development throughout the life cycle i.e. from conception to old age.</p>
<ul>
<li>Child psychologists  focus on infants and children. They are concerned with issues such as whether babies are born with distinct personalities and temperaments, how infants become attached to their parents and caregivers e.t.c.</li>
<li>Adolescent psychologists  specialize in the teenagers. Concerned with puberty, changes in relationships with peers and parents and the search for identity e.t.c.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Life-span psychologists  focus on adult life years. Are concerned with different ways that individuals adjust to partnership and parenting, middle-age, retirement and eventually death.</li>
</ul>
<p>iii) Social psychology: Concerned with understanding the impact of social environments on the individual.</p>
<p>Are interested in: attitude formation and change; social perception; conformity; social roles; prejudice and discrimination; interpersonal perception (forming impressions of others); interpersonal attraction; and aggression.</p>
<p>iv) Personality psychology: Explore the uniqueness of the individual; describe the key elements that provide the foundation for human personalities; and investigate how personality develops, and evolves.</p>
<p>v) Experimental psychology: Primarily conduct research on basic psychological processes  in an attempt to understand the nature and causes of behavior e.g. emotion, sensation, motivation, cognition, perception, learning, memory e.t.c.</p>
<p>vi) Physiological psychology/ neuroscience: Investigates the biological basis of human behavior, thoughts, emotions, memory, disordered behaviors, e.t.c. Biological psychologists explore such things as the association between behavior and drugs, hormones, genes and brain processes.</p>
<p>vii) Clinical and counseling psychology: Involved in the diagnosis and treatment of psychological problems including such things such as developmental disorders, substance abuse, relationship difficulties, vocational and educational problems and behavior disorders.</p>
<ul>
<li>Counseling psychologists: Focus on less serious problems of adjustment e.g. Social or academic adjustment; guidance in career decisions.</li>
<li>Clinical psychologists: Likely to work in mental health clinics, mental hospitals, juvenile and adult courts, medical schools and prisons. They have PhD training in psychological theory, research methods, and techniques of clinical diagnosis and psychotherapy strategies. In their perspectives on causes of psychological problems, they tend to emphasize psychosocial causes e.g. inappropriate learning, faulty attitudes and disturbed interpersonal relationships. They focus on psychotherapy as the best road to improvement.</li>
</ul>
<p>viii) Educational and school psychology:</p>
<p>Educational psychology  involves the study of learning and teaching methods, focusing on ways to improve educational curricula and training teachers.</p>
<p>School psychology  concerned with educating and resolving learning and emotional problems. Often administer and interpret personality, interest and ability tests. Are a valuable resource both for troubled students and for concerned teachers trying to cope with the stresses of classroom problems.</p>
<p>ix) Industrial/ organizational psychology: Uses psychological concepts to make the workplace a more satisfying environment for both employees and management. They work with businesses either as company employees or as consultants, designing programs to: improve group morale; increase job satisfaction; foster better communication within the corporation; enhance productivity; increase workers' involvement in decision making; and improving working conditions.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>They are also concerned with issues such as designing job- training programs and selection of personnel, most suitable for a particular job.</p>
<p>x) Engineering psychology/ human factors psychology: Focuses on the creation of optimal relationships among people, the machines they operate and the environments in which they work. e.g. designing the lighting and instrumentation within the cockpits of sophisticated aircraft to maximize pilot efficiency. In space programs  they have helped to develop optimal/ functional efficiency within the severely limited confines of spacecraft.</p>
<p>xi) Health psychology: Concerned with the relationship between behavioral factors and physical health. It involves the use of psychological principles to promote change in peoples' attitudes and behaviour about health and illness. Deal with diverse areas e.g.</p>
<ul>
<li>Assessing the psychological and physical effects of stress and stress reduction.</li>
<li>Coping strategies for dealing with serious or catastrophic illness.</li>
<li>Evaluating the impact of psychological factors on diseases such as cancer and cardiovascular illness.</li>
<li>Devising ways to test people for susceptibility to disease.</li>
<li>Seeking to identify the factors that motivate people to engage in life- threatening activities e.g. smoking, overeating, under eating.</li>
<li>Using psychological theories and interventions to prevent damaging behaviours and to change to health related behaviours (healthy diet, health checks, self-examination, teeth-brushing, washing of hands etc)</li>
<li>Health-related cognitions-investigating the processes that can explain, predict, and change health and illness behaviours</li>
<li>Processes influencing health-care delivery eg communication, facilitating adherence eg taking medication, preparing for stressful medical procedures</li>
<li>Psychological aspects of illness: looking at the psychological impact of acute and chronic illness on individuals, careers and families.</li>
</ul>
<p>xii) Positive psychology: Study of human behavior aimed at discovering and promoting the positive strengths and attributes that enable individuals to thrive and succeed, to be self - fulfilled and happy. ( positive qualities of an individual: Optimism; courage; work ethics; interpersonal skills; social responsibility; capacity for pleasure; insight e.t.c.</p>
<p>xiii) Forensic psychology: Application of psychological principles and methods to the criminal justice system. It works with the legal, court and correctional systems:</p>
<p>to develop personality profiles of criminals/offender profiling;
to select the jury;</p>
<ul>
<li>present evidence;</li>
<li>give eyewitness testimony;</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>make decisions about disposition of convicted offenders e.g. 1991  Jeffrey Dahmer murdered, dismantled and apparently ate selected body parts from numerous victims;</li>
<li>help law enforcers understand behavioral problems e.g. family conflict, substance abuse;</li>
<li>devise treatment programmes e.g. anger management;</li>
<li>crime prevention; and</li>
<li>assess the risk of releasing prisoners.</li>
</ul>
<p>xiv) Artificial intelligence and connectionism</p>
<p>Artificial intelligence: Researchers develop computer models to simulate human cognitive processes e.g. perceive stimuli, problem  solving, learning and making decisions. Artificial intelligence has been successfully applied to such varied pursuits as the diagnosis of illness and location of deposits of valuable resources such as oil.</p>
<p>Connectionism: Focuses on discovering the neurobiological mechanisms underlying learned associations. It helps to study complex human abilities e.g. learning, problem  solving and perception.</p>
<p>xv) Sports psychology: Works with athletes to help improve their performance by helping them learn to concentrate better, deal with stress and practice more efficiently.</p>
<p>xvi) Individual differences: This is concerned with the ways in which people differ from one another including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Personality</li>
<li>Intelligence</li>
<li>Psychological abnormality</li>
</ul>
<p>xvii) Cross-cultural psychology: examines the role of culture in understanding behaviour, thought, and emotion. Cross cultural psychologists compare the nature of psychological processes in different cultures, with a special interest in whether or not psychological phenomena are universal or culture specific.</p>

<p>studocu</p>
