<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="psychology-as-a-science">PSYCHOLOGY AS A SCIENCE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychology-as-a-science" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Psychologists gather information by thinking critically about, and scientifically studying the mind/mental processes and behaviour.</li>
<li>This entails the use of scientific research (objective, systematic, and testable).</li>
<li>Scientific research is based on the scientific method, an approach that can be used to discover accurate information.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB: Psychology differs from common sense in various ways:</p>
<ul>
<li>Psychology is a scientific inquiry based on thorough research. Common sense is information gathered from everyday informal observations, knowledge and experiences.</li>
<li>Psychology involves judgments which have been rigorously tested and verified in order to ascertain the truth unlike common sense.</li>
<li>It relies on objective evidence. It is based on observations, theories, and experiments.</li>
<li>It uses logic to reason about phenomena and derive conclusions</li>
<li>It relies on empirical evidence</li>
<li>Information is gathered systematically</li>
<li>It relies on precision, accuracy, and lack of bias</li>
<li>Assumptions are validated or refuted</li>
<li>Common sense does not use analytical methods, which are crucial to decision making and judgment</li>
<li>Psychology rules out the metaphysical but common sense allows us to believe that ghosts and angels are causal agents of events in our lives.</li>
<li>Clarity and precisioncommonsense notions about behaviour tend to be vague and ambiguous. In contrast, the scientific approach requires that people specify exactly what they are talking about.</li>
<li>Scientists subject their ideas to empirical tests. They also scrutinize one anothers findings with a critical eye. They demand objective data and thorough documentation before they accept ideas. In contrast, commonsense analyses involves little effort to verify ideas or detect errors.</li>
<li>The scientific approach yields more accurate and dependable information than casual analyses and armchair speculation.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="steps-in-a-scientific-investigation">Steps in a scientific investigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#steps-in-a-scientific-investigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-conceptualizing-a-problem-specifying-a-problem">a) Conceptualizing a problem/specifying a problem<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-conceptualizing-a-problem-specifying-a-problem" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the question one wants to answer, or the puzzle one wants to solve. It entails narrowing down and zeroing in on what one wants to study.</li>
<li>Theories can be used in conceptualizing a problem (a theory is a coherent set of interrelated ideas that helps to make predictions and explain data).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-formulating-a-testable-hypothesis">b) Formulating a testable hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-formulating-a-testable-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>A hypothesis is a tentative idea that might explain a set of observations.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>May also be defined as specific assumptions or predictions that can be tested to determine their accuracy.</li>
<li>A hypothesis is also a statement of the possible differences, relationships or causes between two or more variables or concepts.</li>
<li>Hypotheses are derived from existing theories, previous research, or personal observations and experiences.</li>
<li>To be testable, hypotheses must be formulated precisely, and the variables under study must be clearly defined.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nb-types-of-hypothesis">NB: Types of hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb-types-of-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-alternative-hypothesis">i) Alternative hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-alternative-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>States that there are significant differences or relationships between variables.</li>
<li>It is denoted by <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{H}_{1}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">1</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s"></span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
<li>Alternative hypothesis are of two types:</li>
<li> Directional alternative hypothesis - shows the effect of the independent variable on the dependent variable eg there is a positive and significant relationship between the qualification of teachers and students achievement in science subjects</li>
<li> Non-directional alternative hypothesis - does not give the direction of effect e.g. there are differences between boys and girls; attitude affects performance etc</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-null-hypothesis">ii) Null hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-null-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>It is denoted by <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mn>0</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{H}_0</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">0</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s"></span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
<li>It is stated to show that there is no effect/difference/relationship between the variables</li>
<li>Any difference or relationship is merely due to chance or error.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB: Researchers achieve these clear formulations of hypotheses by providing operational definitions of the relevant variables.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="c-operational-definition-of-terms">c) Operational definition of terms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c-operational-definition-of-terms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>It establishes precisely what is meant by each variable in the context of the study.</li>
<li>It makes concepts clearer by being very specific about what observational measurements define them.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="d-select-the-research-method-and-design-of-the-study">d) Select the research method and design of the study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#d-select-the-research-method-and-design-of-the-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>The research method chosen will depend to a large degree on the nature of the question under study.</li>
<li>The various methods - experiments, case studies, surveys, naturalistic observation etc have advantages and disadvantages.</li>
<li>The researcher has to ponder the pros and cons and then select the strategy that appears to be the most appropriate and practical.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Once researchers have chosen a general method, they must make detailed plans for executing their study-time, number of people needed, where the participants will come from etc (participants or subjects are the persons or animals whose behaviour is systematically observed in a study).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="e-collect-the-data">e) Collect the data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#e-collect-the-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Researchers use a variety of data collection techniques, which include observation, questionnaires, interviews, psychological tests, physiological recordings (blood pressure, muscle tension, brain activity) examination of archival record (census, economic, medical, legal, educational, and business records) etc</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="f-analyze-the-data">f) Analyze the data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#f-analyze-the-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Researchers analyze the raw data to decide whether their hypotheses have been supported or not.</li>
<li>Statistics play an essential role in the scientific enterprisein understanding what the data mean.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="g-draw-conclusions">g) Draw conclusions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#g-draw-conclusions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Based on their statistical analysis, conclusions are drawn.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="h-report-the-findings">h) Report the findings<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#h-report-the-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Researchers should share their findings with one another and with the general public.</li>
<li>They should therefore write up a concise summary of the study and its findings.</li>
<li>Typically they prepare a report that is delivered at a scientific meeting and submitted to a journal for publication.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="research-methods">RESEARCH METHODS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#research-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-experimental-research">1) Experimental research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-experimental-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It allows psychologists to determine behaviors causes.</li>
<li>Its a research method in which an investigator manipulates a variable under carefully controlled conditions and observes whether any changes occur in a second variable as a result.</li>
<li>It is a research method in which a group of participants receive a treatment and another group does not. Participants in the experimental group receive the treatment whereas participants in the control group do not. The participants are then observed to determine whether the treatment makes a difference in their behavior.</li>
<li>There is an independent variable (IV) whose presence is manipulated by the experimenter so that its effects can be determined.</li>
<li>The measured result is called a dependent variable (DV). Its presence presumably depends on the independent variable.</li>
<li>Subjects should be assigned to the experimental or control group on a chance or random basis in such a way that each person has an equal chance of being placed in any group (random assignment).</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>This technique is appropriate in establishing cause-effect relationships
For example to determine whether T.V. violence causes aggressive behavior- T.V. violence would be considered as the IV and the childs behavior will be the DV.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantage">Advantage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This approach comes as close as none other known in establishing causality-permits conclusions about cause and effect relationships between variables.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A number of variables are not amenable to experimental manipulation e.g. age, gender, etc.</li>
<li>It would be unethical to manipulate some variables e.g. withholding treatment from a patient and exposing him/her to danger</li>
<li>Experimental designs are difficult to conduct in natural settings/they are often conducted in an artificial environment. Subjects may react to artificiality and not perform normally.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cont-research-methods">Cont. Research methods<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cont-research-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-observation">2) Observation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-observation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Whereby ongoing behaviour of individuals is recorded with as little interaction between the observer and subjects as possible.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Forms:</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>i) <strong>Naturalistic</strong> observation where researchers observe people in real life settings. They do not try to alter behaviour or the environment; they simply record what they see.
ii) <strong>Laboratory</strong> observation: researchers observe and record behaviour in a controlled environment, such as a laboratory.</p>
<ul>
<li>Both kinds of observation provide valuable descriptions of behaviour.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Facilitates observation of subjects in their natural settings.</li>
<li>Development can be observed in various stages.</li>
<li>Crucial for determining how children/adults function in their everyday life.</li>
<li>Researcher does not impose himself/herself on the subject.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Observation does not explain why people behave the way they do, though the observers may suggest interpretations (does not explain case-effect/why events are happening).</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
6 studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>An observers presence can alter behaviour. When people know they are being watched, they may act differently.</li>
<li>There is a risk of observer bias: the researchers tendency to interpret data to fit expectations or to emphasize some aspects and minimize others.</li>
<li>There are ethical problems if consent is not obtained.</li>
<li>Data obtained from observation is vulnerable to bias and distortion.</li>
<li>Emotions, prejudices and values may influence the way that behaviours and events are observed.</li>
<li>Observation may also be time consuming.</li>
<li>Does not allow for experimental control.</li>
<li>Does not provide information about the internal psychological events and experiences of subjects, their thoughts, beliefs, fantasies etc</li>
<li>May be inefficient in studying behaviour if it occurs infrequently or irregularly in the subjects life.</li>
<li>One may have to wait for a long time before the behaviour of interest is emitted.</li>
<li>The information got may not be generalized to other subjects.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-case-study">3) Case study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-case-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is an in depth study of an individual, a group of individuals or an institution</li>
<li>A case study is frequently used when there is a new phenomenon about which not much is known or very rare events in which few subjects can be found.</li>
<li>Case studies provide significant amounts of descriptive information and they can also provide some explanatory information about why as well as what.</li>
<li>One can use interviews, direct observation of the subject, examination of records, and psychological testing to collect the data.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>They offer useful in-depth information</li>
<li>Useful in exploring behavior</li>
<li>Flexibility i.e. the researcher is free to explore avenues of inquiry that arise during the course of the study</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Disadvantages</p>
<ul>
<li>Time consuming</li>
<li>Can be quite costly</li>
<li>Subject drop-out may occur if the study is carried out over an extended period</li>
<li>They may provide information about the development of a single person, but not how the information applies to general people</li>
<li>Case studies cannot explain behavior with certainty or make strong causal statements because there is no way to test their conclusions</li>
<li>Case studies are highly subjective-it is easy for investigators to see what they expect to see in case study research (researchers may focus selectively on information that fits with their expectations).</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Correlational studies</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>A correlational study seeks to determine whether a relationship exists between variables/ phenomena.</li>
<li>Correlations are expressed in terms of direction i.e. positive or negative and magnitude (degree)</li>
<li>Two variables that are positively correlated increase or decrease together. For example, studies show a positive or direct correlation between televised violence and aggression. That is, children who watch more violent television tend to fight more than children who watch less violent television.</li>
<li>Two variables have a negative or inverse correlation if as one increases the other decreases. Studies show a negative correlation between amount of schooling and the risk of developing dementia (mental deterioration) due to Alzheimers disease in old age. In other words, the less education, the more dementia.</li>
</ul>
<p>Advantage</p>
<ul>
<li>Correlations enable us to predict one variable in relation to another</li>
</ul>
<p>Disadvantage</p>
<ul>
<li>Correlational research does not allow us to place clear cause and effect labels on variables. To investigate cause and effect, researchers turn to the experimental method.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Surveys</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Researchers use questionnaires or interviews to gather information about specific aspects of participants' behaviour.</li>
<li>The survey questions should be unambiguous and clear.</li>
<li>The people surveyed must be selected with great care.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Surveys are a relatively inexpensive way to collect a lot of data fairly quickly.</li>
<li>They provide data that can be used to formulate or test a hypothesis.</li>
<li>They make it relatively easy to collect data on attitudes, opinions, beliefs, preferences etc from large samples of subjects</li>
<li>Surveys are often used to obtain information on aspects of behaviour that are difficult to observe</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>They depend on self-report data-intentional deception can distort participants' verbal reports about their behaviour.</li>
<li>The value of survey is limited by what people are capable of reporting accurately.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-clinical-method">6) Clinical method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-clinical-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It involves a researcher and a subject at a time</li>
<li>It entails observation and careful questioning and allows for flexibility</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantage">Advantage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Questions can be individualized for each subject</li>
<li>It facilitates a detailed observation/questioning</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Conclusions arrived at depend on the interviewer's questioning ability</li>
<li>Results obtained may require verification by other researchers</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="heredity-and-environment">HEREDITY AND ENVIRONMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#heredity-and-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-determinants-of-human-development">The determinants of human development<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-determinants-of-human-development" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Although genes play a role in human behaviour, they alone do not determine who we are.</li>
<li>Genes exist within the context of a complex environment and are necessary for an organism to exist.</li>
<li>The interaction of nature and nurture, genes and environment, influences every aspect of mind and behaviour. Neither factor operates alone.</li>
<li>Heredity and environment operate together to produce a persons intelligence, temperament, height, weight, personality, abilities etc</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="environment">Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Refers to all the surrounding conditions and influences that affect the development of living things.</li>
<li>It includes the food we eat, the air we breathe, and the many different physical and social contexts we experiencethe towns and cities we live in, our relationships with our parents, peers, teachers; our continuing interactions at work, at home, at play etc</li>
<li>The term <strong>nurture</strong> is used to describe an <strong>organisms environmental experiences</strong>.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="genes">Genes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The term <strong>nature</strong> is used to describe an <strong>organisms biological inheritance</strong>.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fertilization">Fertilization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fertilization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>One begins life as a single celled organism, and develops into a human being made up of trillions of cells.</li>
<li>According to Poole, Warren, and Nunez (2007) an individual starts to form when the male and female reproductive cells (gametes) combine to form a single cell called a <strong>zygote</strong>. This process is referred to as <strong>conception/fertilization</strong>. This is facilitated by <strong>meiosis</strong>-the process of gamete formation which involves halving the number of chromosomes from 46 to 23. <strong>Mitosis</strong> takes place after the zygote has been formed.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>A zygote has 23 pairs of chromosomes.</li>
<li>22 are called autosomes and contain most of the genetic information for the new individual.</li>
<li>The 23rd pair, the sex chromosomes determines the sex.</li>
<li>Zygotes containing 2 X chromosomes develop into females.</li>
<li>Those containing one X and one Y chromosome develop into males</li>
<li>NB: Sex determination is influenced by the male gametes.</li>
<li>The nucleus of each human cell contains chromosomes.</li>
<li>Chromosomes are threadlike strands of DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid) molecules that carry genetic information.</li>
<li>With the exception of the sex cells (sperm and eggs), every cell in humans contains 46 chromosomes.</li>
<li>These chromosomes operate in 23 pairs, with one chromosome of each pair coming from each parent.</li>
<li>Each chromosome in turn contains thousands of biochemical messengers called genes.</li>
<li>Genes are DNA segments that serve as key functional units in hereditary transmission.</li>
<li>Every person has two genes for each characteristic governed by heredity.</li>
<li>When genes combine to determine our characteristics, some genes are dominant over others.</li>
<li>According to the dominant-recessive genes principle, if one gene of a pair is dominant, and one is recessive, the dominant gene exerts its effects, overriding the potential influence of the recessive gene.</li>
<li>A recessive gene exerts its influence only if both genes of a pair are recessive.</li>
<li>Genotype is a person's genetic heritage, the actual genetic material.</li>
<li>Phenotype is the way an individuals genotype is expressed in observable, measurable characteristics. Phenotype includes physical characteristics (such as height, weight, and eye colour), and psychological characteristics (such as intelligence and personality).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="twins">Twins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#twins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Identical twins (monozygotic) develop from a single fertilized ovum, and are therefore identical in genetic makeup.</li>
<li>Any differences between them may be due to environmental influences.</li>
<li>Fraternal twins (dizygotic) develop when two separate eggs are fertilized simultaneously.</li>
<li>Fraternal twins are no more alike in genetic makeup than any two siblings born to a pair of parents at different times.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chromosomal-abnormalities-and-the-resultant-behaviour">Chromosomal abnormalities and the resultant behaviour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chromosomal-abnormalities-and-the-resultant-behaviour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>

studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nb">NB:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Chromosomes exist as pairs so that the proper number (46) is maintained</li>
<li>When chromosomes exist as a trio, this condition is known as trisomy.</li>
<li>If chromosomes are not paired, this condition is known as monosomy.</li>
<li>If sex-determining chromosomes are involved in trisomy, this is referred to as sex chromosome trisomy.</li>
<li>If sex determining chromosomes are involved in monosomy, this is known as sex chromosome monosomy.</li>
<li>When other chromosomes are involved in trisomy, this is referred to as autosomal trisomy; and if involved in monosomy, this is known as autosomal monosomy.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="examples-of-trisomy-and-monosomy">Examples of trisomy and monosomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examples-of-trisomy-and-monosomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-sex-chromosome-monosomy">1. Sex chromosome monosomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-sex-chromosome-monosomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Turners syndrome</strong>: disorder in females in which either an x chromosome is missing, making the person xo instead of xx, or the second x chromosome is partially deleted.</p>
<p><strong>Characteristics</strong>: short in stature, webbed neck, may be infertile, poorly developed secondary sex features e.g. breast size, sexual dysfunction in adulthood, broad chest with widely spaced nipples, perceptual and cognitive deficiencies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-sex-chromosome-trisomy">2) Sex chromosome trisomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-sex-chromosome-trisomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>a) Klinifelters syndrome</strong>: (named after Dr. Harry Klinifelter in 1942): disorder whereby males have an extra x chromosome, making them XXY instead of XY.</p>
<p><strong>Characteristics</strong>: underdeveloped testes, enlarged breasts, reduced body hair, and possible infertility, poor muscular and motor development, speech and language disorders, taller than average, intelligence is within normal limits, though IQ scores may be on the lower end of the scale.</p>
<p><strong>b) Trisomy XYY (Supermale Syndrome)</strong></p>
<p>A sex chromosomal disorder that affects malescharacterized by an extra Y chromosome.</p>
<p><strong>Characteristics</strong>: heavy beards, unusual tallness, mild mental retardation, particularly in language development, a violent antisocial personality.</p>
<p><strong>c) Trisomy XXX (triple X syndrome)/Super female Syndrome</strong></p>
<p>Individuals with this condition have an extra X chromosome and are female.</p>
<p><strong>Characteristics</strong>: delay in speech and language development, lack of coordination, poor academic performance and immature behavior, taller than average, poor muscular and motor development, neuro-maturational delay, timid personality. Sexual development is usually normal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-autosomal-monosomy">3) Autosomal monosomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-autosomal-monosomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Has not been observed in humans because the embryo cannot proceed beyond the early developmental stages.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-autosomal-trisomy">4) Autosomal trisomy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-autosomal-trisomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>a) Down syndrome /Trisomy 21: caused by a deviation on the 21st pair of chromosomes; the individual may have 47 chromosomes. It was discovered in 1866 by a British doctor Langdon Down.</p>
<p>Characteristics: small hands, large tongue, severe mental retardation, heart defects, added risk of leukemia, flattened facial features, short stature, poor motor coordination, retarded expressive language, developmental delays in terms of the onset of sitting, standing, walking and speech development.</p>
<p>NB: the probability of giving birth to an infant with Downs syndrome increases with the age of the mother.</p>
<p>b) Trisomy D and E</p>
<ul>
<li>Involve an extra chromosome to pairs 13 and 15</li>
</ul>
<p>Characteristics: deficient forebrain development, small or absent eyes, absence of olfactory tracts.</p>
<p>NB: Trisomy E leads to cardiac problems and kidney defects.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="perception">PERCEPTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#perception" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sensation-and-perception">Sensation and Perception<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sensation-and-perception" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sensation is the process through which signals from the environment are detected by the sensory receptors, encoded into nervous impulses and then passed on to the brain.</li>
<li>Sensation is the process by which the senses gather information about the environment. The sense organs gather information that makes us consciously aware of our surroundings. The sensory process and the nervous system make us aware of our surroundings. The basic unit in the nervous system is called the neuron. Neurons are found in sensory tissue, along nerve pathways, in the spiral column and in the brain itself. In the sensory tissue there are specialized cells called receptors. They receive information from inside the body and from the environment.</li>
<li>When sensory information is received by the receptors it goes through the process of transduction. This is the process that converts physical energy into neural messages which are electro-chemical in nature. The neural messages then travel along nerve pathways to the peripheral nervous system, then to the brain. The brain registers what is sensed.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="general-characteristics-of-sensation">General characteristics of sensation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-characteristics-of-sensation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sensation begins with a stimulus in the environment. A stimulus is any form of physical energy a light wave, heat, odor, sound, pressure or taste. The stimulus excites the nervous system. The stimulus first excites one of the sensory receptor cells. The receptor cells transform the physical energy into neural impulse. This transformation is called transduction. Each sensory system is specialized to respond to a different type of stimulation.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-many-senses-do-humans-have">How many senses do Humans have?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-many-senses-do-humans-have" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Aristotle had identified five senses vision, hearing, smell, taste, touch. Today scientists have recorded ten or more senses. The sensory receptors of all these senses perform the same general function of receiving stimulus from the environment, converting it into neural energy and relaying it to the brain. In the brain the neural impulses are experienced as light, smell, taste, sound etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examples-of-senses">Examples of senses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examples-of-senses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>A) Chemical senses</p>
<p>i) Smell (olfactory system - the sensory system for smell)</p>
<ul>
<li>The receptors for smell are situated in each nasal cavity in a small cell-packed area called the olfactory epithelium.</li>
<li>The receptors collect information regarding romatica (odors) to the brain.</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Taste (gustatory system - the sensory system for taste)</p>
<ul>
<li>This is closely related to smell.</li>
<li>The receptors reside in the tongue in the back of mouth and throat</li>
<li>Receptors are in the packs of taste buds</li>
<li>They collect chemical information carried in food, and temperature consistency of the food.</li>
<li>They can sense four major tastes, sweet, sour, salty and bitter.</li>
</ul>
<p>B) The skin (tactile)</p>
<ul>
<li>Our skin is the largest sense organ.</li>
<li>It protects us from the environment, holds in body fluids, and regulates our internal temperature.</li>
<li>It has numerous nerve receptors distributed in varying concentrations throughout its surface.</li>
<li>Skin receptors give rise to the sensations of pressure, temperature, and pain.</li>
</ul>
<p>C) Body senses</p>
<p>i) Kinesthetic sense</p>
<ul>
<li>Kinesthetic sense relays information about body position and movement.</li>
<li>It relies on sensory receptors located in muscles, tendons and joints.</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Vestibular</p>
<ul>
<li>The vestibular sense is concerned about equilibrium.</li>
<li>It enables us to catch ourselves when we trip, and helps us to run without falling.</li>
<li>It helps to maintain balance. The sensory receptors are in the inner ear, in the three semi-circular canals filled with fluid. Each time a movement is made the liquid shifts.</li>
</ul>
<p>D) Hearing</p>
<ul>
<li>The hearing sense is the most important in terms of human communication. The energy force called sound comes to us in waves. These waves are molecules of air travelling at approx 750 miles an hour.</li>
<li>The tone and pitch of sound depend on the number of waves travelling per second. The strength and loudness of the sound is determined by the height of the waves.</li>
<li>Of all our senses, hearing is most sensitive in terms of discrimination. We can distinguish many voices, musical notes, friendly and unfriendly greeting.</li>
<li>The receptor cells are found in the cochlea which is a bony inner ear structure.</li>
</ul>
<p>E) The visual sense</p>
<p>26</p>
<p>Humans are visual animals. The visual sense is the most important in terms of human perception. About 80% of the information is collected by the eyes. The eye has 70% of the receptors. We think and reason using visual images and symbols.</p>
<p>The basis of all visual stimuli is light. Light is an electromagnetic force that travels through space and approx 186,000 miles per second. Light travels from sources like the sun, candles, and electric bulbs. This energy travels in waves which hit surfaces at different intervals and with different force. The waves of light travel in varying lengths and intensities. All the wave lengths of light appear as colorless white. When these wave lengths strike the surface they scatter and each appears as a different color or hue.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="perception">Perception<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#perception" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Process of selection, organization and interpretation of sensory information/input in addition to the way we respond (meaning we attach to sensation). For perception to occur there must be sensory stimulation to which we attach meaning.</li>
<li>The brains process of organizing and interpreting sensory information. Perception is a creation of the brain; it is based on input extracted from sensory organs, such as the eyes, ears, and nose.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="relationship-between-sensation-and-perception">Relationship between sensation and perception<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#relationship-between-sensation-and-perception" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sensation is the process of inputting of information through the senses.</li>
<li>Perception is the process through which organization of the information understanding of what has been sensed occurs (what has been seen, heard, smelled, tasted, and touched).</li>
<li>Sensation and perception are important in psychology because they are our link with the outside world.</li>
<li>The information we gather has a great effect on our psychological functioning. Sensation and perception are part of the psychological process by which we gain knowledge about the world. The process of acquiring knowledge is called cognition and it starts from sensation and perception which enable learning, memory, thinking and language development.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="importance-of-perception">Importance of perception<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#importance-of-perception" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Sensation is a part of the perceptual process. Through perception, we integrate sensations into percepts. A percept is the simplest meaningful pattern. Thus perception helps us generate meaning /make sense of our sensations.</li>
<li>Our ability to perceive enables us to obtain the information we need to survive.</li>
<li>We are constantly bombarded by many stimuli that compete for attention. We therefore need to discriminate what is important and what can fulfill our needs.</li>
<li>The sensory and perceptual process helps us gather information about our environment. This information assists in goal/need attainment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="perceptual-illusions">Perceptual illusions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#perceptual-illusions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>They demonstrate the ways we use a variety of sensory cues to create perceptual experiences that may or may not correspond to what is out there in the real world.</li>
<li>They occur when we <strong>misperceive</strong>- seeing things that are not there in reality e.g. Car/train stops but you still feel like the movements are continuing; the moon illusion-the full moon appears to be much smaller when overhead than when looming over the horizon; when driving at night you think the moon is following you.</li>
<li>Illusions are incorrect- by understanding how we are fooled into seeing something that isnt there, psychologists can gain insight into the ways that perceptual processes work.</li>
<li>Perceptual illusions typically occur because the stimulus contains misleading cues that distort the perceived size or orientation of common objects, giving rise to inaccurate or impossible perceptions.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examples">Examples:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examples" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thresholds">Thresholds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thresholds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Human beings require a certain minimum stimulation of the hearing sense in order to perceive sound. This minimum stimulation is known as the <strong>absolute threshold</strong>-the smallest amount of stimulus needed in order to notice that the stimulus is present at all. All our sense organs have an individualized absolute threshold.</li>
</ul>
<p>The absolute thresholds for human beings are quite low. The absolute threshold for:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p><strong>Light</strong>: is a candle light 30 miles away on a dark, clear night</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>For smell</strong>: a drop of perfume diffused throughout a three-room apartment</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Hearing</strong>: the ticking of a watch from 6 meters(20 feet) in very quiet conditions</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Taste</strong>: one tea spoon of sugar in two gallons of water (45 litres x 2 = 10 litres of water)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Touch</strong>: the wing of a fly falling on your cheek from a height of one centimeter.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>As we sense various stimuli in the environment, we require the ability to detect small differences between these stimuli. For example, a tea taster must be able to detect minute differences in flavor between various qualities of tea. The minimum difference that can be detected with respect to our sensory system is referred to as <strong>difference threshold or just noticeable difference (jnd)</strong> -the smallest change in stimulation that you can detect-e.g. change from no stimulation to stimulation, or change from less stimulation to more. How much stronger must a sound become before you notice that it has grown louder?</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Suppose Kahlo was painting while the sun was setting; at what point would she notice that it had gotten darker in the room, and she should either adjust the way she was painting patterns of light, or call it quits for the day? <strong>Jnd is the size of the difference in a stimulus property (e.g. brightness of light) needed for the observer to notice a difference</strong> (Kosslyn and Rosenberg, 2006).</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>NB: Subliminal perception</strong>: stimuli that are too weak for us to notice and vet but are able to affect us (below threshold) e.g. advert, musical background etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sensory-adaptation">Sensory adaptation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sensory-adaptation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>30</p>
<ul>
<li>This is defined as the diminishing sensitivity to unchanging stimuli. For example, when you walk into a darkened movie theater, you can hardly see anything except the image on the screen. Gradually however, your eyes become accustomed to the dark; when you enter a room that is freshly painted initially the odor is strong and uncomfortable but with time one adapts to the odor; mortuary attendants; hospital smells; and noise - initially continuous noise is annoying. But with time the person adapts to it and becomes unaware of it.</li>
<li>Thus our senses automatically adjust to the overall, average level of stimulation in a particular setting.</li>
<li>When confronted by a great deal of stimulation, they become much less sensitive than when the overall level of stimulation is low.</li>
<li>Adaptation allows our senses to be keenly attuned to environmental conditions without becoming overloaded.</li>
<li>When you enter a hushed room, you are able to hear the faintest sound. But when you go out onto a busy street at rush hour, the noise of street traffic would seem deafening, even painful, unless your ears adapted and became less sensitive to noise.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="perceptual-organization">Perceptual organization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#perceptual-organization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>We use structures to categorize various stimuli, resulting in meaningful perceptions.</li>
<li>These structures are Figure and Ground Perception and Perceptual Grouping.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-figure-and-ground-perception">a) Figure and Ground Perception<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-figure-and-ground-perception" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the principle by which we organize the perceptual field into stimuli that stand out (figure) and those that are left over (ground)-the background, which must be distinguished in order to pick out any figure..</li>
<li>In order to recognize an object, we need to first perceive this object as being separate from its surroundings. For example- when we write some words on a piece of paper, the words are perceived as being distinct from the paper. The words assume the characteristics of a figure, while the paper becomes a mere background.</li>
<li>NB: when figure and ground are similar, the figure is said to be camouflaged (animals in the jungle, as well as armies the world over, have long taken this advantage of the perceptual system</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-perceptual-grouping">b) Perceptual Grouping<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-perceptual-grouping" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This requires bringing some order in terms of grouping stimuli together. This ordering is governed by the following principles.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="1-proximity-things-that-are-near-one-another-seem-to-belong-together-e-g-we-see-xxx-xxx-as-two-groups-and-xx-xx-xx-as-three-groups-even-though-both-sets-have-the-same-total-number-of-x-marks-xoxoxoxoxoxoxo">1) Proximity: Things that are near one another seem to belong together- e.g. we see XXX XXX as two groups, and XX XX XX as three groups even though both sets have the same total number of X marks; XOXOXOXOXOXOXO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-proximity-things-that-are-near-one-another-seem-to-belong-together-e-g-we-see-xxx-xxx-as-two-groups-and-xx-xx-xx-as-three-groups-even-though-both-sets-have-the-same-total-number-of-x-marks-xoxoxoxoxoxoxo" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
