<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="xoxoxoxoxoxoxo">XOXOXOXOXOXOXO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#xoxoxoxoxoxoxo" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the above example, we perceive rows, because the principle of proximity creates the impression that the X's and the O's are related due to their nearness to each other.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li><strong>Similarity</strong>: Things that look alike tend to be grouped together. So, for example, we see XXXxxx as two groups;</li>
</ol>
<p>XXXXXXXXXX</p>
<p>OOOOOOOOOO</p>
<p>In this example, similar shapes are grouped together in the perceptual process.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li><strong>Continuity</strong>:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>We tend to perceive smooth continuous patterns rather than discontinuous ones.</li>
<li>Marks/things that fall along a smooth curve or a straight line tend to be grouped together</li>
<li>Examples:</li>
<li>Eg 1: We see ---as a single line, not four separate dashes; and we see --- --- as two separate lines all eight of the dashes do not fall on the same plane.</li>
<li>Eg 2:</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th>OXOOOXXOOOXXOOOXXOOOXXOOOXX</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>OOXOOXOOXOOXOOXOOXOOXOOXOOXOO</td></tr><tr><td>OOOXXOOOXXOOOXXOOOXXOOOXXOO</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the above example, we perceive a continuous wave pattern made up of X's, due to the influence of the principle of continuity.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li><strong>Closure</strong>:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>People often group elements to create a sense of closure or completeness.</li>
<li>We tend to close any gaps in a figure, thus enabling one to perceive disconnected parts as being complete.</li>
<li>So a circle with a small section missing will still be seen as a circle.</li>
</ul>
<p>32</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Simplicity/Good form: people tend to group elements that combine to form a good figure.</li>
</ol>
<p>e.g.</p>
<p>This could be viewed as a complicated 11-sided figure, but given the preference for simplicity, one is more likely to see it as a rectangle and triangle.</p>
<p>NB: The process of perception therefore requires proper organization of various stimuli.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="perceptual-constancies">Perceptual constancies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#perceptual-constancies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Tendency to perceive objects as relatively stable and unchanging despite changing sensory information. Once we have formed a stable perception of an object, we can recognize it from almost any position, at almost any distance, under almost any illumination. A white house looks like a white house by day or by night and from any angle. We see it as the same house.</li>
<li>Perception of the characteristics of objects (such as their shapes and colours) as the same even though the sensory information striking the eyes changes.</li>
<li>This enables us to recognize objects without being deceived by their shape, size, brightness, or color.</li>
<li>There are four main perceptual constancies;</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-shape-constancy">a) Shape constancy:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-shape-constancy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the tendency to perceive familiar objects as having a constant shape in spite of the viewing angle.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="example">Example:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>a dinner plate is viewed as circle, even when it is tilted and the retinal image is oval; a rectangular door will project a rectangular image on the retina only when it is viewed directly from the front. From any other angle, it casts a trapezoidal image on the retina, but is not perceived as having suddenly become a trapezoidal door.</li>
<li>Stabilization occurs not in the eyes, but in the brain, and is fundamental to the ability to recognize objects and know how to interact with them.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-size-constancy-this-is-the-tendency-to-perceive-an-object-as-having-a-constant-size-in-spite-of-the-object-being-far-away-from-us">b) Size constancy: This is the tendency to perceive an object as having a constant size in spite of the object being far away from us.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-size-constancy-this-is-the-tendency-to-perceive-an-object-as-having-a-constant-size-in-spite-of-the-object-being-far-away-from-us" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="example">Example:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>We perceive a car/aeroplane as big enough to carry people, even when we see it from afar.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="c-brightness-constancy">c) Brightness Constancy:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c-brightness-constancy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>33</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>This is the tendency to perceive objects as having consistent brightness even when their illumination varies.</li>
<li>Recognition that an object retains the same degree of brightness even though different amounts of light fall on it.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Example:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>White paper reflects 90 percent of the light falling on it, while black paper reflects only 10 percent in well-illuminated conditions. In bright sunlight conditions, the black paper may reflect 100 times more light than does the white paper indoors (McBurney&amp;Collings, 1984), yet it still looks consistently black.</li>
<li>Regardless of whether you are reading a textbook indoors or outdoors, the white pages and the black print do not look any different to you in terms if the whiteness or blackness.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>d) Color Constancy:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>This refers to the tendency to perceive familiar objects as having consistent color even when the illumination changes.</li>
<li>Hence we tend to see familiar objects as keeping their colours, regardless of information that reaches the eye.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Example:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>A yellow dress looks yellow when observed during the day or evening, when the amount of light diminishes, it looks white, but it is still yellow.</li>
<li>If you own a red car, you will see it as red whether it is on a brightly lit street or in a dark garage, where the low light may send you a message it is closer to brown or black than red.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Factors that influence human perception</strong></p>
<p>Various factors contribute to perceptual differences such as;</p>
<p><strong>1) Characteristics of the stimulus object</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>A stimulus object is anything that draws the attention of our sense organs.</li>
<li>Objects that are vivid in terms of possessing a sharp distinction between the figure and background are easiest to perceive.</li>
<li>Colorful objects as well as those with patterns are powerful stimulus objects.</li>
<li>Conversely, objects that are dull-colored, vague and lacking in contour are easily ignored.</li>
<li>In class, teachers are encouraged to apply vividness, color and pattern to educational stimuli in order to enhance learning.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>2) Culture</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>This is a very crucial factor because we are all subject to both cultural and ethnic forces.</li>
<li>Demands of different cultures lead to greater emphasis on certain senses.</li>
<li>Eg the Maasai who depend on herding tend to perceive individual differences in what others perceive as a herd of virtually identical animals; wine experts can distinguish subtle differences in the flavor of different vintages that most of us could not detect.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>It has been proposed that Africans perceive round objects better than those with straight lines, while Caucasians prefer the opposite of this.</li>
<li>In the late sixties, Deregowskis study on Malawians revealed that the group involved perceived colored photographs better than the black and white pictures. The implication of this was that the Malawians lived in a colorful culture, hence their tendency to perceive the colored photographs satisfactorily.</li>
<li>Turnbulls study on the Pygmies of the Congo (1969) showed that the subjects under study had problems of size constancy. The pygmies were shown some buffaloes at a distance and asked to explain what they saw. They responded that they could see insects. The implication of these results was that pygmies lived in a heavily forested area where trees prevented long distance perception, hence these people did not have ample opportunity to develop size constancy.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-context-characteristics">3) Context characteristics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-context-characteristics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The term context characteristics refers to the setting in which perception occurs.</li>
<li>This implies that a person may be exposed to a stimulus and the way he/she perceives this stimulus will depend on the circumstances under which it is presented.</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th>Example:</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>12 13 14 15</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>In this example, the number 13 can also be viewed as a B depending on the context of application.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-selective-attention-and-individual-values">4) Selective Attention and Individual Values<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-selective-attention-and-individual-values" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the tendency to focus on a specific stimulus and to ignore all the other competing stimuli.</li>
<li>Usually, the stimulus that captures our attention most is the one that is most vivid, colorful or loud.</li>
<li>In addition, stimuli containing patterns or emotional potential will enhance selective attention.</li>
<li>One example we can consider is that of a picnicker and a naturalist in a park. The naturalist will be able to perceive the sounds of birds and animals in the bush as opposed to the picnicker who will generally concentrate on conversation sounds. These two individuals will be in the same environment but will perceive different stimuli due to their special interests, needs and values.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-characteristics-of-the-perceiver">5) Characteristics of the Perceiver<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-characteristics-of-the-perceiver" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Individual differences influence the way we appreciate various stimuli. For example, an individuals state of intelligence will influence his/her response to a stimulus.</li>
<li>One illustration of this is the effect of an examination stimulus. Bright students view examinations as a challenge to prove their proficiency in answering questions, while the dull students look at examinations as a major threat.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>35</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Our state of mind can also influence the way we perceive.</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>For example, a person who is very sick in hospital may not appreciate watching his/her favorite television program. A person who is intoxicated with alcohol will be prone to risky sexual behaviour because of the drinks potential to reduce inhibitions. In this case, risky sexual behaviour is transformed into an acceptable temptation.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="7">
<li>Motivation</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Our desires and needs shape our perceptions.</li>
<li>People in need are more likely to perceive something that they think will satisfy their need. E.g. a mirage-people lost in the desert have visual fantasies of an oasis over the next dune.</li>
<li>Research has found that when people have not eaten for some time, they perceive vague images as being related to food (Morris and Maisto, 2005).</li>
</ul>
<ol start="8">
<li>Expectations</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Preconceptions about what we are supposed to perceive may also influence perception by causing us to delete, insert, transpose, or otherwise modify what we see.</li>
<li>This phenomenon of perceptual familiarization reflects a strong tendency to see what we expect to see even if our expectations conflict with external reality.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="9">
<li>Cognitive style</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>
<p>As we mature, we develop a cognitive stylea personal way if dealing with the environmentand this also affects how we see the world (Morris and Maisto, 2005).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Psychologists distinguish between two general approaches that people use in perceiving the world.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Field dependence: people who are field dependent tend to perceive the environment as a whole and do not clearly delineate in their minds the shape, colour, size, or other qualities of individual items. If field dependent people are asked to draw a human figure, they generally draw it so that it blends into the background.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Field independent: are more likely to perceive the elements of the environment as separate and distinct from one another and to draw each element as standing out from the background.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<ol start="10">
<li>Personality</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>A number of researchers have shown that our individual personalities influence our perceptions (Greenwald, 1992).</li>
<li>For example, normal college students were compared to depressed students in their ability to identify words related to depression.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Depressed students were faster at identifying adjectives describing personality traits they commonly thought about, such as quiet, withdrawn, hesitant and timid; than they were at identifying adjectives related to traits they rarely thought about, such as extrovert, lively and bold.</li>
<li>These findings suggest that not only personality, but possibly the presence of a personality disorder, may influence personality.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="11-learning">11) Learning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#11-learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Everybody is born with innate perceptual tendencies. Learning is that experience which results from interaction with objects and events, people, places. Learning guides the unfolding of innate abilities. This requires that the person be active in the world to be perceived. The world itself must be a rich one with things to the sounds to hear objects to manipulate. If the environment is impoverished e.g. a dark room, solitary confinement. The person suffers sensory deprivation which affects perception. Severe sensory deprivation to the visual sense causes severe damage to receptor cells in the retina. People who have been blind from birth and who regain sight as adults have difficulty recognizing shapes, faces. They still tend to recognize them by touch. They are unable to learn constancies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="12-perceptual-adaptation-or-constancy">12) Perceptual adaptation or constancy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#12-perceptual-adaptation-or-constancy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>People perceive the world as unchanging even when objects are seen from changing shapes and sizes. We perceive the cupness of a cup, the doorness of a door, the goatness of a goat. We perceive constancy of shape, size, colour and brightness. Perceptual constancy relies on experience and knowledge of a particular sensory image before it is modified. It helps us to see the world as relatively unchanging and stable. A coin is round, door is rectangular not trapezium.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="13-subjective-experience">13) Subjective experience<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#13-subjective-experience" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>People see what they want to see, hear what they want to hear. There is a strong influence of selective perception on human behaviours. This is the process of perceiving the environment on the basis of individual needs, values, expectations even if the interpretation is incorrect. People have a way of selecting various pieces of information organizing them in accordance to private motivations depending on one's life experiences, the influence of cultures, hunger, need for love, loneliness, need for power all influence perception.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="14-past-experience">14) Past experience<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#14-past-experience" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Past experience keeps the world stable which is positive. It also has a negative effect as it keeps people from expanding their perceptual horizons. The tendency to perceive the environment in determined ways is called and perceptual set. The perceptual set influences attitudes, values, interests and other personal judgments. Once a perceptual set has been formed it is difficult to change it even when situations and circumstances change. The perceptual set interferes with relationships between parents and children husbands and wives, teachers and students. The perceptual set is influenced by the people  friends, teachers, relatives, advertisers, governments (brain washing, propaganda). People who want to</p>

<p>break from old perceptual sets experience problems. Changes occurs so fast that people have little time to adapt to new perceptual sets  this causes maladjustment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="extrasensory-perception-esp">Extrasensory Perception (ESP)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#extrasensory-perception-esp" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>According to Santrock (2000) ours eyes, ears, mouth, nose, and skin provide us with sensory information about the external world.</li>
<li>Our perceptions are based on our interpretation of this sensory information.</li>
<li>However, some people claim they can perceive the world through something other than normal sensory pathways- this means that they are able to engage in sensation without the intervention to the five senses.</li>
<li>The field of ESP is nowadays left to experts popularly known as parapsychologists, who study the paranormal. The term paranormal refers to phenomena beyond the normal, as you can deduce from my examples above.</li>
<li>ESP is perception that occurs without the use of any known sensory process.</li>
</ul>
<p>Parapsychologists have classified various phenomena as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Telepathy: thought transference from one person to another at a distance. Mind to mind communication, where one person sends thoughts to another, who may or may not possess the ability to respond. Telepathy also includes the ability to perceive another's thoughts.</li>
<li>Clairvoyance: Perceiving remote events, that are not in sight, such as sensing that a relative is sick in hospital, how much money is in a sealed envelope, number on a card, a person at a movie theater sensing a burglar breaking into his/her house at that moment etc</li>
<li>Precognition: Perceiving future events-knowing events before they happen. For example, a fortune teller might claim to see into the future and tell you what will happen to you in the coming year; an astrologer might predict that a major earthquake will take place etc</li>
<li>Psychokinesis, which refers to the ability to perform a mental operation to influence a material body or energy system. It is the mind-over the matter phenomenon of being able to move objects without touching them, such as mentally getting a table to rise off the floor or shattering the glass merely by staring at it. A person with this gift can move objects using thoughts.</li>
<li>Teleportation: ability to relocate oneself to places of one's wish.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="eps-100-learning">EPS 100: LEARNING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#eps-100-learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Behaviorists define learning as an enduring change in behavior potentiality, which occurs as a result of reinforced practice (B. Skinner, 1953; Gredler, 2001)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Focuses on observable behaviors.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>This definition has key concepts the first one being that learning is a change in behavior. This key element in the definition informs us that a person who did not know how to swim and learns how to swim has experienced a change in behavior from a non-swimmer to a swimmer. Likewise a person who did not know how to use a certain formula to arrive at answers to mathematics problems and learns how to use it and now computes the math problems using the formula has learned. That learning is a relatively permanent change means that learned behaviors are not easily lost a good example being learning to perform motor tasks like learning how to ride a bicycle or drive a car is never forgotten. Similarly, people learn how to read and write and they never forget. Learning occurs under conditions of reinforcement means that people must be motivated by certain incentives to learn. Learning occurs under conditions of practice means that for any behavior to be learned a lot of practice is a necessary condition</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cognitive psychologists define learning as the mental activity by means of which knowledge, skills, habits, attitudes and ideas are acquired, retained and utilized, resulting in the progressive adaptation and modification of behavior and conduct.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-behavior-changes-are-not-considered-as-learning">What behavior changes are not considered as learning?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-behavior-changes-are-not-considered-as-learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Behavior changes that as a result of maturation, organic deterioration, damage to the nervous system are not considered learned because they are not acquired under conditions of reinforced practice and also because they are related to deterioration of adaptive behavior.</li>
<li>If a behavior is caused by the effect of fatigue, pain or sensory adaptation it is not considered learned because the changes are temporary and the person reverts back to normal behavior once the transitory state shifts.</li>
<li>Also changes of behavior caused by ingestion of alcohol and other drugs do not fall under the category of learned behaviors because they are temporary states.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="research-in-learning">Research in Learning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#research-in-learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Psychology is scientific and therefore explains behavior through empirical observation of data.</li>
<li>In order to explain how learning occurs and to develop principles of learning psychologists conduct research. Such research is meant to discover the conditions that facilitate learning and to develop theories.</li>
<li>The researchers in this area have used animals such as dogs, rats, cats, pigeons, chimpanzees and where research ethics allow human beings have been used.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="why-are-animals-used-in-learning-research">Why are animals used in learning research?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-are-animals-used-in-learning-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>39</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The researchers argue that the humans and animals learn in much the same way and therefore the research findings from animal studies can be generalized to human beings to explain how learning occurs and also to discover the general principles as well as the conditions under which learning occurs.</p>
<p>Researchers who use animals in learning research also argue that animals experience and genetic background can be precisely controlled and as such they can find out the extent to which genetic heredity determines learning as well as the environmental conditions that influence what is learned.</p>
<p>They also argue that animals' lives are shorter and therefore many generations of animals can be studied.</p>
<p>Human beings cannot be subjected to laboratory conditions and studied the way animals are done as it would raise ethical concerns.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-learning">Types of learning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-behavioral-view-of-learning">The behavioral view of learning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-behavioral-view-of-learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The behavioral approach includes two views: namely, classical conditioning, and operant conditioning.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="conditioning">CONDITIONING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It involves learning associations between events that occur in an organism's environment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classical-conditioning">Classical Conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classical-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>It grew out of a tradition that can be traced back to Aristotle (384  322 B.C), who believed that learning depended on contiguity  occurrence of events close together over and over again, till they become associated (e.g. lightning and thunder). Later, when only one of these (a stimulus) occurs, the other will be remembered too (response).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In the early 20th century, the research of Ivan Pavlov Russian Scientist - (1849  1936) stimulated worldwide scientific research interest in the study of associative learning.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Experiments in classical conditioning were first conducted by Ivan Pavlov (1849  1936).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pavlov was a Russian scientist who studied the dogs' digestive system and who received a Nobel Prize for his work in 1903. While working with the dogs in the laboratory Pavlov noted the followings; that when the dogs heard the footsteps of the experimenter or even saw the food bowl it salivated. He argued that this salivation was a learned behavior because only food should cause an organism to salivate.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-5b845c6f.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>Contiguity plays a major role in another more complex learning process. It has been called by various names i.e. <em>classical conditioning</em>, <em>respondent conditioning</em>, <em>signal learning</em>.</li>
<li><em>Conditioning is a psychological process that causes an organism/person to respond in a particular way to a stimulus</em> (anything that elicits a behavior or response) i.e. sights, sounds, smell e.t.c. the senses receive from the environment.</li>
<li><em>Learning through conditioning is therefore similar to learning through association</em> i.e. the process where a previously neutral stimulus is able to elicit a certain response.</li>
<li><em>Pavlovs experiment on a dog</em> illustrates how learning takes place through conditioning.</li>
<li>Using these 3 elements  <em>food</em>, <em>the salivation and the bell</em>, <em>Pavlov demonstrated that a dog could be conditioned to salivate after hearing the bell</em>.</li>
<li>Pavlov began by ringing a bell and recording the dogs response. There was no salivation.</li>
<li>Then he fed the dog with a piece of meat (S). The response was salivation (R).</li>
<li>The <em>food</em> in this case was an <em>unconditional stimulus</em> (US) since it brought forth an automatic response of salivation. The <em>salivation</em> was an <em>Unconditioned Response</em> (UR), again because it occurred <em>naturally</em>/automatically.</li>
<li>He noted that a neutral stimulus (such as a bell) at first prompted no response.</li>
</ul>

<p>UCS is paired with neutral stimulus. UCS produces UCR.</p>
<ul>
<li>Pavlov's experiment showed that if a previously neutral stimulus is paired with an unconditioned stimulus, the neutral stimulus becomes a conditioned stimulus.</li>
<li>He did this by contiguous pairing of the sound/tone with food.</li>
<li>He rang the bell and then quickly fed the dog. After repeating this several times, the dog began to salivate after hearing the sound, but before receiving the food.</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classical-conditioning-after-conditioning">Classical Conditioning After Conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classical-conditioning-after-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

CR</p>
<p>Neutral stimulus is now the conditioned stimulus. It produces CR, salivation, which is similar to the UCR produced by the Hot Dog.</p>
<ul>
<li>The response of salivating after the bell, now a CR was very similar to the original response to the food.</li>
<li>The bell had become a CS that could bring forth salivation by itself.</li>
<li>What Pavlov demonstrated was how stimulus-response associations  the basic building blocks of learning  are formed by events in an organisms environment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="basic-processes-in-classical-conditioning">Basic processes in classical conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#basic-processes-in-classical-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Acquisition-the formation of a new conditioned response tendency</li>
<li>Stimulus contiguity-association between two events: Learning process must be done in such a way that that it is possible to associate unconditional responses. Pavlov thought that the key to Classical conditioning is the pairing of stimuli.</li>
<li>The process must be repeated severally until the association is formed fully.</li>
<li>The association must be reinforced by use of reward or punishment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-classical-conditioning">Principles of Classical Conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-classical-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-the-principle-of-reinforcement">1) The principle of reinforcement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-the-principle-of-reinforcement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>When the dog is put in the experimental situation and the tone is sounded and then food is given, this constitutes reinforcement. The food is a pleasant event presented to the dog and it constitutes positive reinforcement. Content should be sequenced to ensure high</p>
<p>44</p>
<p>correlation and integration of all learning tasks  continuity in curriculum content. Learning of skills and concepts is easily reinforced.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-the-principle-of-extinction">2) The principle of extinction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-the-principle-of-extinction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Extinction refers to the dying of the learned response, which occurs when reinforcement is withdrawn. It can also be described as the gradual weakening and disappearance of a conditioned response tendency. This occurs when a dog has been conditioned to associate the tone or bell with presentation of food and after some time the food is not delivered. When the experimenter rings the bell or sounds the tone without giving the dog food, the learnt behavior disappears.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-the-principle-of-spontaneous-recovery">3) The principle of spontaneous recovery<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-the-principle-of-spontaneous-recovery" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Spontaneous recovery is the reappearance of a learnt response without the reintroduction or reinforcement. It occurs if a dog that was initially conditioned to associate the sound of the bell or tone with the presentation of the food is taken through the process of extinction. It is then given some resting time where no bells are heard. If this dog is returned to the experimental situation again and the bell is sounded, it will salivate even if no food is given. However, this response dies very fast if reinforcement is not reintroduced.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-the-principle-of-generalization">4) The principle of generalization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-the-principle-of-generalization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Generalization refers to responding to stimuli, which is similar to the original stimuli as long as both are reinforced. This occurs when the experimenter reinforces the dog with food every time he sounds different types or tones or bell sounds. In this training, the dog learns that every time there is a bell sound, whatever the type, there will be food. Therefore, the dog learns to respond to similarities. So when there is a tone similar to the one it was conditioned to, it salivates.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-the-principle-of-discrimination">5) The principle of discrimination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-the-principle-of-discrimination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Discrimination refers to learning to pick out the differences in the stimuli and therefore responding to a very specific stimulus. It occurs when an organism that has learned a response to a specific stimulus does not respond in the same way to new stimuli that are similar to the original stimuli. This occurs when a dog has been conditioned to respond to a particular stimulus. If the experimenter introduces other stimuli, he does not accompany them with the reinforcement. As a result, the dog learns to pick out the differences in stimuli and to respond to very specific ones. Discrimination training takes a much longer time and more trial to establish.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="operant-instrumental-conditioning">Operant/instrumental conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#operant-instrumental-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is a form of learning in which voluntary responses come to be controlled by their consequences.</li>
<li>It is associated with B.F Skinner (1904  1990)</li>
<li>Skinner (1953), used boxes, known as Skinner boxes to study learning.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>The box had a level/ bar at one corner, which was connected to a food magazine containing food pellets.</li>
<li>A hungry rat would be put in the Skinner box. Hunger would motivate the rat to move. Each movement was called a trial.</li>
<li>The rat would accidentally touch the bar and operate the food magazine, which would release the food pellets.</li>
<li>Eventually, the rat learnt to associate the bar- pressing behavior with food.</li>
<li>Therefore, the bar- pressing behavior was the learnt response which was accompanied by the food which was the reinforcement event.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-55e91974.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reinforcement-contingencies-in-operant-conditioning">Reinforcement contingencies in Operant Conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reinforcement-contingencies-in-operant-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Skinner also studied the different kinds of relationships between behaviors and their consequences, which he termed as behavioral contingencies i.e.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Positive reinforcement: Process of increasing the frequency or duration of a behavior as the result of presenting a reinforcer/ rewarding stimulus e.g. a teacher's positive comments or compliments that encourage students. Good grades, tasty meals, scholarships, promotions, attention and flattery are all positive reinforcers.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Negative reinforcement: The frequency of a response increases because it is followed by the removal of an aversive (unpleasant) stimulus. It involves a favorable outcome that strengthens a response tendency. e.g.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>A father nags at his son to do homework. He keeps nagging. Finally, the son gets tired of having to hear the nagging and does his homework. The son's response (doing homework) removed the unpleasant stimulus (nagging).</li>
<li>A student chronically misbehaves in class and is sent to the Dean of Students. Next day, the misbehavior occurs sooner and you send the student out again. The intent was to stop misbehavior; however, it was negatively reinforced.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Punishment: presenting aversive/unpleasant stimuli to decrease undesirable behavior. It occurs when an event following a response weakens the tendency to make that response. Punishment typically involves presentation of an aversive stimulus (e.g. spanking). However, punishment may also involve removal of a rewarding stimulus (e.g. taking away a child's TV-watching privileges).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Primary and secondary reinforcers</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Primary</p>
<p>They are events that are inherently reinforcing because they satisfy biological needs eg water, pain avoidance, and temperature regulation.</p>
<p>Physiological needs are unlearned and survival related</p>
<p>Secondary</p>
<p>They are learned or acquired. They are not related to survival. The person acquires these needs as he/ she interacts with other people. For example, good grades, praise, attention, approval, success, flattery, applause, and affection.</p>
<p>Schedules of reinforcement</p>
<ul>
<li>A schedule of reinforcement is a specific pattern of presentation of reinforcers over time.</li>
<li>There are two schedules of reinforcement:</li>
<li>Continuous: Every instance of a desired behavior/designated response is reinforced.</li>
<li>Partial: Reinforcing a response only part of the time. Skinner (1953) developed partial reinforcement timetables that determine when a response will be reinforced.</li>
</ul>
<p>4 Kinds of Partial Reinforcement schedules according to B.F. Skinner</p>
<p>i) Fixed Ratio (FR):-Predictable reinforcement</p>
<p>-A reinforcer is given after a fixed number of desired responses, regardless the amount of time it takes e.g. praising the student after every fourth correct response.</p>
<p>ii) Variable Ratio(VR):-Unpredictable reinforcement</p>
<p>-It is one in which the number of behaviours required for reinforcement is unpredictable, although it is certain that the behaviours will eventually be reinforced- e.g. reinforcing the <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mtext>th</mtext></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{\text{th}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8491em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8491em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">th</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> correct response, then <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>5</mn><mtext>th</mtext></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5^{\text{th}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8491em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8491em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">th</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>, then <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>14</mn><mtext>th</mtext></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">14^{\text{th}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8491em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">4</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8491em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord mtight">th</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> etc.</p>
<p>iii) Fixed Interval(FI): -Predictable reinforcement</p>
<p>-Based on set time intervals e.g. giving students a final exam or giving a weekly test.</p>
<p>iv) Variable Interval (VI): -Unpredictable reinforcement</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>

<ul>
<li>It is based on variable time intervals e.g. giving surprise quizzes. The VI is effective for maintaining a high rate of behaviour, while being highly resistant to extinction.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intelligence">INTELLIGENCE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="definition">Definition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>According to McDevitt and Ormrod(2002) theorists define intelligence in many different ways, but most agree it has several distinctive qualities:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is adaptive, such that it can be used flexibly to respond to a variety of situations and problems.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It involves learning ability: People who are intelligent in particular domains learn new information and behaviours more quickly and easily than people who are less intelligent in those domains.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It involves the use of prior knowledge to analyze and understand new situations effectively.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It involves the complex interaction and coordination of many different mental processes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is culture-specific: what is intelligent behaviour in one culture is not necessarily intelligent behaviour in another culture.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Intelligence is therefore the ability to benefit from experiences and therefore modify future behaviours to accomplish new tasks successfully.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Intelligence is also the ability to abstract or adjust to ones environment. This involves the ability to learn, solve problems, memory, reasoning etc.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="components-of-intelligence">Components of Intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#components-of-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Ability to reason/Ability to think abstractly</li>
<li>Ability to learn/learn from experience</li>
<li>Ability to adapt to environment</li>
<li>Ability to solve problems</li>
<li>Understand and learn complex material</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-mental-quickness">6. Mental quickness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-mental-quickness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Because of its general nature, intelligence integrates cognitive functions such as perception, attention, memory, language, or planning.</li>
<li>On the basis of this definition, intelligence can be reliably measured by standardized tests with obtained scores predicting several broad social outcomes such as educational achievement, job performance, health, and longevity.</li>
<li>To allow for comparison of test scores among persons, L. Terman devised the concept of intelligence quotient (IQ):</li>
</ul>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">Q</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo><mo></mo><mn>100</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm {I Q} = (\mathrm {M A} / \mathrm {C A}) \times 1 0 0</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">IQ</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">MA</span></span><span class="mord">/</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">CA</span></span><span class="mclose">)</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin"></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mtext>mental&nbsp;age</mtext></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm {M A} = \text {mental age}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">MA</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">mental&nbsp;age</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mtext>chronological&nbsp;age</mtext></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm {C A} = \text {chronological age}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">CA</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">chronological&nbsp;age</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">Q</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi>M</mi><mi>A</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>C</mi><mi>A</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo></mo><mn>100</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm {I Q} = \frac {M A}{C A} \times 1 0 0</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">IQ</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0463em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3603em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span><span class="mord mathnormal">A</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.10903em;">M</span><span class="mord mathnormal">A</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s"></span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin"></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<ol>
<li>Chronological Age (CA)-This is the age from birth.</li>
<li>Mental Age (MA)- This is the level of mental development determined by standardized intellectual mental tests for various age groups. If a child has a CA=3 and MA=4, he is capable of doing work of an older child (4 years) so deemed bright.</li>
<li>IQ (Intelligent Quotient)-This is the rate of mental development or speed calculated as follows;</li>
</ol>
<p>Using the example in number 3) the child's IQ would be</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">Q</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>4</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mn>3</mn><mo></mo><mn>100</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{IQ} = 4 / 3 \times 100</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8778em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">IQ</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">4/3</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin"></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span></span></span></span></span>  which is about 133 so very bright</p>
