<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mental-retardation">Mental retardation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mental-retardation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Children with mental retardation show developmental delays in most aspects of their academic and social functioning.</li>
<li>They exhibit below-average intelligence and deficits in adaptive behaviour</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes-of-mental-retardation">Causes of mental retardation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-mental-retardation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Birth injuries leading to brain damage</li>
<li>Prolonged labour (difficult birth) leading oxygen deprivation i.e. anoxia hence brain damage</li>
<li>Inherited deficiencies/Chromosomal deficiencies-Down syndrome</li>
<li>Inbreeding (marrying relatives who share genes e.g. most royal families produce feeble minds, no intellectuals)</li>
<li>Ingestion of toxic substances by mother when expectant e.g. alcohol, drugs, eating soil which could have lead.</li>
<li>Malnutrition especially lack of proteins leading to fewer brain cells</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Age of mother  past 40 years, children of low intelligence especially if it is the first child.</li>
<li>Diseases such as venereal diseases e.g. syphilis affects babys nervous system, German measles</li>
<li>Radiation that is to say X rays</li>
<li>Parental neglect or an extremely impoverished and unstimulating home life</li>
<li>Iodine deficiency leads to cretins i.e. short babies with big heads and low IQ because glandular system is affected</li>
<li>RH factor due to incompatibilities of blood between couples. The antibodies from mothers blood fight the perceived stranger/enemy in the womb i.e. the foetus antigens that carries fathers blood cells leading to either miscarriage, still birth or mental retardation. Only about 2% of mothers have negative rhesus factor.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="structure-of-intelligence">Structure of intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structure-of-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It consists of:</p>
<ol>
<li>Abstract intelligenceability to deal with formulas, symbols in the brain e.g. statistics, calculations without anyone even noticing</li>
<li>Concrete intelligenceability to deal with objects mechanically e.g. plumbers, mechanics, carpenters without necessarily understanding the reasons behind what they do. Such people are just practical as opposed to theoretical people.</li>
<li>Social intelligenceability to deal with social situations and people appropriately and effectively e.g. not making irrelevant jokes, wrong talk, embarrass othersnot being a social idiot</li>
<li>Emotional intelligenceability to identify, assess or control the emotions of oneself, others or groups. This can be self-perceived or perceived by others as they look at how one deals with people and situations.</li>
<li>Practical intelligenceknowing what to do in situations and going ahead and doing it (implementers) e.g. going back to school in order to get promotions in future.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="theoretical-perspectives-of-intelligence">Theoretical perspectives of intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#theoretical-perspectives-of-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-charles-spearman-british-psychologist">1) Charles Spearman (British psychologist)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-charles-spearman-british-psychologist" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Used a psychometric approacha theoretical perspective that portrays intelligence as a trait (or set of traits) on which individuals differ.</li>
<li>Spearman noted positive correlations to several different cognitive tests. He speculated that there must be a general mental factor that explained the consistent performance  Intelligence g-factor.</li>
<li>He also noted that there are certain inconsistencies regarding performance in certain areas. He speculated that there were specialized traits that denoted this occurrenceintelligence s-factor.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>51</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>I.e.</p>
<ul>
<li>g-generalized ability, contributes to all intellectual activities/used in a wide variety of tasks and content domains</li>
<li>s-specialized abilities - a number of narrow abilities involved in executing particular tasks</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-raymond-cattell-and-john-horn">2) Raymond Cattell and John Horn<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-raymond-cattell-and-john-horn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>They came up with two distinct aspects of intelligence:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-fluid-intelligence">i) Fluid intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-fluid-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Largely the result of inherited biological factors.</li>
<li>The ability to perceive relationships and solve relational problems of the type that are not taught and are relatively free of cultural influences.</li>
<li>It involves the ability to acquire knowledge quickly and thereby adapt readily to new situations.</li>
<li>It peaks in late adolescence and begins to decline in the early 20s.</li>
<li>It relates more to novel tasks, especially those that require rapid decision making and are largely nonverbal in nature.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-crystallized-intelligence">ii) Crystallized intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-crystallized-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>It depends on both fluid intelligence and experience and so it is influenced by both heredity and environment.</li>
<li>It continues to increase throughout childhood, adolescence, and most of adulthood.</li>
<li>The ability to understand relations or solve problems that depend on knowledge acquired from schooling and other cultural influences.</li>
<li>It is more important for familiar tasks, especially those that are heavily dependent on language and prior knowledge</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2758629b">3) Sternbergs Triarchic Model<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2758629b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Spear Sternberg proposed that intelligence is comprised of 3 aspects:</p>
<p>a) Analytical intelligence - refers to mental processes e.g. ability to learn how to do things, acquire new knowledge, solve problems, and carry out tasks effectively. Most intelligence tests assess analytical intelligence.</p>
<p>b) Creative intelligence: ability to adjust to new tasks, use new concepts, combine information in novel ways, respond effectively in new situations, gain insight, and adapt creatively.</p>
<p>c) Practical intelligence: people who score high are good at finding a multitude of solutions to practical and personal problems. They make the most of their talents by seeking out situations that match their skills, shaping those situations so they can make optimal use of their skills, and knowing when to change situations to better fit their talents.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Thurstones Multiple intelligences theory/ multifactor theory of intelligence</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Number abilitybeing able to perform simple arithmetic problems rapidly and without errors</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Word fluencyability to produce words rapidly and appropriately</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Verbal factorability to find similarities and differences or contrasts in the meaning of words</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Memoryability to memorize rapidly and accurately by rote</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Reasoningability to find underlying concepts</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Spatial relationsability to identify spatial relations in pictured objects (what can fit where and perform pictorial representations)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Perceptual speedability to recognize likeness and differences in visual forms rapidly and accurately</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Howard Gardners Eight Intelligences</p>
</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="measurement-of-intelligence">Measurement of Intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measurement-of-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Intelligence testing is important to help in designing of the right educational/academic programmes/curriculums and consequently matching them to the job market.</li>
<li>It also helps educators know where children fit in the educational programmes</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages-of-group-iq-tests">Disadvantages of group IQ tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages-of-group-iq-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Group tests tend to emphasize speed, verbal factors and reading comprehension to a greater extent than do individual tests</li>
<li>Motivation and fatigue not noticeable</li>
<li>An uninterested, anxious, confused child may get a poor than is typical of his performance.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intelligence-tests">Intelligence tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intelligence-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>They include:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-weschler-intelligence-scale-for-children-wisc">i) Weschler Intelligence Scale for Children-WISC<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-weschler-intelligence-scale-for-children-wisc" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>WISC III: is designed for children and adolescents aged 6-16</li>
<li>It contains 13 sub-tests, each involving either verbal responses or object manipulation.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-stanford-binet-intelligence-scale">ii) Stanford-Binet Intelligence Scale<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-stanford-binet-intelligence-scale" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Can be used with people ages 2 through adulthood</li>
<li>The individual being assessed is asked to perform a wide variety of tasks, mostly involving verbal material and response (e.g. defining vocabulary words, finding</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>logical inconsistencies in a story, or interpreting proverbs) but a few involving manipulation of concrete objects (e.g. cardboard boxes, buttons or blocks).</p>
<p>It yields an overall IQ score, plus more specific scores in verbal reasoning, abstract/visual reasoning, quantitative reasoning, and short-term (i.e. working) memory.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-universal-nonverbal-intelligence-test-unit">iii) Universal Nonverbal Intelligence Test (UNIT)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-universal-nonverbal-intelligence-test-unit" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Designed for children and adolescents ages 5-17</li>
<li>The UNIT consists of six sub-sets involving memory or reasoning regarding visual stimuli.</li>
<li>Its contents was chosen from objects and symbols presumed to be universal across all cultures</li>
<li>Instructions are given entirely through gestures, pantomime, and modeling and the child responds by either pointing or manipulating objects.</li>
<li>Ideal for children who have hearing impairments, children who have language-related learning disabilities, and children for whom English is a second language</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="problems-with-intelligence-tests">Problems with intelligence tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#problems-with-intelligence-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>They are not culture-fair (items used/asked about are not within the immediate environment of test takers)</li>
<li>They fail to get a native inborn ability</li>
<li>A childs performance and resulting IQ are greatly affected by his home background and other social factors.</li>
<li>The tests leave untouched many important aspects of personality such as interests, motives, attitudes, social adaptability etc</li>
<li>Group tests do not allow for clarifications</li>
<li>Test takers may not be motivated enough</li>
</ul>
<p>ExampleWhich of the following is taken before the main dish?ice cream, coffee, soup, tea. The answer depends on ones culture as well as socio-economic factors.</p>
<p>To resolve this, tests that were appropriately normed (culture-fair tests) were introduced that take into consideration the kinds of experiences that the children have had.</p>
<p><strong>NB</strong>: There cannot be culture-free tests since all tests are developed in a culture (test developers and takers are from a certain culture)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="conditions-that-must-be-fulfilled-for-a-test-to-be-considered-culture-fair">Conditions that must be fulfilled for a test to be considered culture-fair<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conditions-that-must-be-fulfilled-for-a-test-to-be-considered-culture-fair" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Test items must be familiar/similar to those in the environment (dont tell Nairobi/urban children to classify wild animals since they only see kittens, dogs, horses)</li>
<li>Language of the test must be well understood by test takers</li>
<li>Test takers must be well motivated to take it otherwise the differences in the scores may not be easy to interpretis it due to IQ, boredom, demotivation etc</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="determinants-of-intelligence-factors-affecting-intelligence">Determinants of Intelligence/ factors affecting intelligence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#determinants-of-intelligence-factors-affecting-intelligence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="evidence-for-hereditary-influences">Evidence for hereditary influences<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#evidence-for-hereditary-influences" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-heredity-role-of-genes">1) Heredity (role of genes)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-heredity-role-of-genes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Studies done show that if you separate siblings and bring them up in different environments, then check their IQ, there is a high correlation between IQ levels of family members. The closer the blood relationship, the greater the correlation.</li>
<li>Twin studies with identical twins have produced results that show the role of heredity. There is a high degree of concordance when identical twins are reared together (almost a positive perfect correlation 0.8). If not reared together, the environment plays a role and reduces correlation. In a review of twin studies, Bouchard and McGue (1981) found these average (median) correlations:</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Correlations of twins IQs</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Identical twins raised in the same home</td><td>.86</td></tr><tr><td>Identical twins raised in different homes</td><td>.72</td></tr><tr><td>Identical twins raised in the same home</td><td>.60</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>MacDevitt and Ormrod (2002) concur that the correlation of .72 indicates that identical twins raised in different environments tend to have very similar IQ scores. These twins are more similar to each other than are fraternal twins raised in the same home.</p>
<ul>
<li>Adoption studies: Adopted children share a similar environment with their adoptive parents; they share a similar genetic make-up with their biological parents. When researchers obtain IQ scores for adopted children, and for both their biological and adoptive parents, they typically find that the childrens IQ scores are more highly correlated with the scores of their biological parents than with the scores of their adoptive parents (Low IQ correlations reported between adopted /foster children and foster parents).</li>
<li>Advocates of heredity include Sir Francis Galton, and Arthur Jensen. Galton looked at the top scientists in Britain and found out that they came from families where parents were intellectually great. He therefore concluded that genius is inherited and recommended for selective breeding (geniuses marrying geniuses) to improve the human race.</li>
<li>Kallikak had an affair with a mentally retarded girl and they gave birth to a mentally retarded child. He later married a normal woman and their children were not retarded. He therefore concluded that mental retardation is inherited (sample too small for generalization, there could have been other causes not studied).</li>
</ul>
<p>56</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Jensen mated fast learning rats with fast learning ones and slow with slow ones. The offspring of fast learning ones learnt to go through a blind maze faster than those of slow learning rats. He also gave tests to both white and black children and found out that black children scored consistently low grades than the whites. He therefore concluded that in the development of intelligent, heredity outweighed the environment (socio-economic factors??)</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Measures of information processing speed correlate with IQ scores. Speed of processing depends on neurological efficiency and maturation, which are genetically controlled.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-evidence-of-environmental-influences">2) Evidence of environmental influences<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-evidence-of-environmental-influences" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Studies of identical twins brought up under different environments (stimulating vs impoverished one) - their IQ, height, weight is measured (ideally if living together all these would be same since they are identical!). When brought up separately the correlations between IQ was 0.4, height 0.8, weight 0.67, scholastic aptitude that depends on IQ was 0.4. Heredity affected only height but the rest were determined by the environment. If heredity was that powerful, it could have defeated environment and produced high correlations for all measures.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Sex - some studies have reported that boys are superior to girls in mathematical, scientific and mechanical aspects though this could also be an aspect of gender socialization/cultural conditioning hence attitudes rather than biological differences. Girls are reported to be better in verbal task.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Race? - no evidence of differences in IQ due to race</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Stimulating home environment: Stimulating home environments  those in which parents frequently interact with their children, make numerous learning and reading materials available, encourage the development of new skills, use complex linguistic structures in conversation  and are associated with higher IQ scores in children (heredity may produce intelligent children, but more intelligent parents provide more stimulating environments).</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\diamond</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> Effects of early nutrition: Severe malnutrition, either before birth or during the early years of life, can limit neurological development and thereby have a long-term influence on cognitive development. Poor nutrition in the early years is associated with lower IQ scores, poor attention and memory, and lower school achievement.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="motivation">MOTIVATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#motivation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-terms">Definition of terms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-terms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Motivation- the process that initiates directs and sustains behaviour to satisfy physiological or psychological needs or wants.</li>
<li>Also defined as the driving force or impetus behind behavior, thoughts and actions.</li>
</ul>
<p>Motives: Needs or desires that energize and direct behaviour toward a goal.</p>
<ul>
<li>Inborn motives are those that are acquired naturally beginning in early childhood, for example, the need for food, water, shelter and oxygen.</li>
<li>Acquired motives are those that are developed through the interaction of an individual with a given environment. For example, the need for social approval is learnt from interacting with other people.</li>
</ul>
<p>Homeostasis- The tendency of the body to maintain a balanced internal state with regard to oxygen level, body temperature, blood sugar, water balance and so forth. When this state is disturbed, a drive is created to restore the balance.</p>
<p>Drive- A state of tension or arousal brought about by an underlying need. It motivates an organism to engage goal-directed behaviour.</p>
<p>a) Primary drives- these are unlearned and serve to satisfy biological/physiological needs e.g. hunger, thirst, sex, sleep, exercise etc. They must be satisfied.
b) Secondary drives- these are acquired needs e.g. need for recognition, achievement, self-esteem, belongingness e.t.c.</p>
<p>Incentive: An external stimulus that motivates behaviour e.g. money, fame etc.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="primary-characteristics-of-motivation">Primary characteristics of motivation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-characteristics-of-motivation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>a. <strong>Activation</strong> - the initiation of motivated behaviour. It involves taking the first steps required to achieve a goal.
b. <strong>Persistence</strong> - the faithful and continued effort put forth in order to achieve a goal.
c. <strong>Intensity</strong> - the focused energy and attention applied in order to achieve a goal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-motivation">Types of motivation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-motivation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>a) <strong>Intrinsic Motivation</strong> - the desire to perform an act because it is satisfying or pleasurable in and of itself.</p>
<p>E.G.</p>
<ul>
<li>A student working hard in a subject because he/she finds it interesting.</li>
<li>A student reads several books a week because reading is fun.</li>
<li>A person donates large sums of money to a university to fund scholarships for deserving students.</li>
</ul>
<p>b) <strong>Extrinsic motivation</strong> - the desire to perform an act to gain a reward or to avoid an undesirable consequence.</p>
<p>E.G.</p>
<ul>
<li>A person agrees to donate huge sums of money to the university to construct a building, provided it will bear the family name.</li>
<li>A student reads two books a week to avoid losing TV privileges.</li>
<li>A student studying to avoid being kicked out of school.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="characteristics-of-motivated-behaviour">Characteristics of Motivated Behaviour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#characteristics-of-motivated-behaviour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The source of motivation is the need that has to be satisfied for an organism to achieve internal equilibrium. The satisfaction of a need constitutes reinforcement because this action is rewarding in itself.</li>
<li>When a goal is attained, the motivated behaviour is terminated. For example, after we sit the final examination, we are not likely to continue studying since the goal has been achieved.</li>
<li><strong>Motives are internalized</strong>; hence we can only infer them from our behaviour. This means that we are not able to tell accurately what motive a person has by just looking at their behaviour or facial expression.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-motivation-cycle">The Motivation Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-motivation-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-337676ad.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>59</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>When equilibrium is disturbed, a drive is created to restore balance. The drive motivates an organism to engage in behaviour that will satisfy the need, and reduce the tension thus restoring equilibrium/balance.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-motives">Types of motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-primary-motives">a) Primary Motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-primary-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Primary motives are also referred to as <strong>primary needs</strong>, <strong>physiological needs</strong>, <strong>biological needs</strong> and <strong>tissue needs</strong>. They originate from within an organism and are influenced by the biological requirements of an organism.</li>
<li>Primary needs <strong>motivate the behaviour of an organism in directions that lead to their satisfaction</strong>. For example, a hungry animal is motivated to look for food, which is necessary for survival. Prolonged failure to satisfy some of the primary needs can result in impaired health, or death.</li>
<li>All <strong>primary needs are inborn</strong>, meaning that they arise from the organisms inherited structures. Primary needs include the need for water, food, oxygen, rest, asleep, shelter, warmth when cold, coolness when hot, the need to relieve the sex drive, pain and bowel (and bladder) tensions.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB: Primary needs cannot be satisfied permanently. For example, when we have a meal, the state of satisfaction is temporary, since the hunger drive will be renewed in a short while.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-secondary-motives">b) Secondary Motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-secondary-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Secondary motives are those drives that an individual learns through interaction with agents in the environment. They are also referred to as <strong>learned motives</strong>, <strong>acquired motives</strong> and <strong>psychological needs</strong>. They are not basic to the survival of an organism. Examples include the need for fame, prestige, power, material possessions, money, greater knowledge, self-esteem and social approval.</li>
<li>Secondary needs are important to man because they involve <strong>interpersonal relations with other people</strong>. These needs are frequently overwhelmed by the physiological needs as well as obstacles in the environment. All in all, their satisfaction is essential to an individuals healthy development. Frustration of the secondary motives may result in emotional disturbances as well as physical illness because of lack of homeostasis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="c-social-motives">c) Social motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c-social-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>These are motives acquired through <strong>social or cultural experience and interaction with others</strong>. These include <strong>affiliative</strong> and <strong>prestige</strong> motives. These motives are found in social groups like: the peer groups, the family, religious groups and political groups. Within which these groups, an individual strives to be liked, loved, admired, respected and envied.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Socially oriented motives are classified in to two broad groups: Affiliative and prestige motives.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-affiliative-motives">a) Affiliative Motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-affiliative-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Affiliative motives represent the desire for love and belonging. Some examples of these motives are as follows:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Desire to be with other people (gregariousness).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Preference for social interaction with people having common interests</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Desire to have intimate contact with ones family.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Affiliative motives are developed from early childhood and are modified as time goes on. They play the following roles:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>They are helpful in character development. For example, through interaction with other people, a child is able to know which behaviour is socially acceptable and which is not.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Affiliative motives also influence moral learning at home and at school. A child who behaves well is commended, one who errors is reprimanded. Therefore, children will do their best so as to get commendation from parents, teachers and peer group members.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Affiliative motives enhance language development. Through interaction with other people, a child develops language, an essential mode of communication.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Affiliative motives enhance an individuals level of aspiration. For example, children at school work better with the teacher that they like. Such a teacher should know when to commend or reprimand and when to use incentives. All these mould a childs attitude towards school, and therefore his aspirations.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-prestige-motives">b) Prestige Motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-prestige-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Prestige motives are those motives aimed at enhancing the degree to which an individual is admired, respected or envied. They are also known as achievement motives, superiority motives and esteem motives.</li>
<li>Prestige motives are tied to an individuals cultural environment. They encourage one to strive to achieve a certain goal and to be recognized for it.</li>
<li>Prestige motives are responsible for the competitive spirit found in any society in areas like fashion, business and education.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="relevance-of-prestige-and-affiliative-motives-in-a-learning-environment">Relevance of Prestige and Affiliative Motives in a Learning Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#relevance-of-prestige-and-affiliative-motives-in-a-learning-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="prestige-motives">Prestige motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prestige-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>61</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Prestige motives encourage the competitive spirit found in any educational setup. Teachers should therefore place their students in a competitive situation as often as possible with a view to improving the quality of written and oral work.</li>
<li>Competitiveness should be varied so that all students meet with success occasionally.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="affiliative-motives">Affiliative motives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#affiliative-motives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Students should be encouraged to identify with one another and be loyal to the class as a group. The teacher should be perceived as a member of the group.</li>
<li>The teacher should foster the affiliative spirit by delegating authority and responsibility in class and including class members in the planning of classroom activities.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="maslow-hierarchy-of-needs">Maslow Hierarchy Of Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maslow-hierarchy-of-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Maslow (1970) suggests that humans have a hierarchy of needs ranging from lower level needs to higher level needs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The lower level needs are survival and safety related.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maslow believed that humans have specific needs that must be met and that if lower level needs go unmet, we can not possible strive for higher level needs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The Hierarchy of needs shows that at the lower level, we must focus on basic issues such as food, sleep, and safety.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The higher level needs are those for intellectual achievement and self-actualization.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>According to Maslow (1970), the five levels of the hierarchy have the following features:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Level 1: physiological needs, which are biological in nature and are unlearned. These needs must be satisfied before an individual can satisfy the needs pertaining to the next level. The physiological needs are: Need for food, water, oxygen and relief from pain, among others.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Level 2: encompasses safety needs, which are persons desire to feel secure in non-threatening physical and social environments which are learned through an individuals interaction with agents in the environment. Some of the safety needs include the need to save money, insure property, acquire job security and live in an atmosphere of peace.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Level 3: addresses love and belonging needs. It is the need to be loved and accepted by significant others like. For example, the desire for gregariousness and identification with others, in addition to the need to give and receive friendship and affection.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Level 4: covers the esteem needs. This is the need to be outstanding in various aspects of life. They are aimed at enhancing a persons position in a group or society. Examples of esteem needs include the need to achieve success, competence and recognition in areas like business, politics, and academics.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Level 5: entails self-actualization or self-fulfillment. This is the need to become the best one could be. It aims at developing the full potentialities of an individual, in terms of creativity, self-expression, creativity, spiritual cleanliness, and community service. Some of the people who may have reached this level which transcends ordinary experience include:</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Mother Theresa (Community Service).</li>
<li>Mozart (Music)</li>
<li>Jesus (Religion)</li>
<li>Mahatma Gandhi (Politics)</li>
<li>Martin Luther King (Civil Rights)</li>
<li>Dos Santos Pele (Sports)</li>
</ul>
<p>Throughout our lives, we work toward achieving the top of the pyramid, self-actualization, or the realization of all of our potential. As we move up the pyramid, however, things get in the way which slow us down and often knock us backward. Imagine working toward the respect and recognition of your colleagues and suddenly finding yourself out of work and homeless. Suddenly, you are forced backward and can no longer focus your attention on your work due to the need for finding food and shelter for you and your family.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="emotions">EMOTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emotions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>According to Morris and Maisto (2005) the ancient Greek rationalists thought emotions, if not held in check, would wreck havoc on higher mental abilities such as rational thought and decision making.</li>
<li>In the past, psychologists too viewed emotion as a base instincta vestige of our evolutionary heritage that needed to be repressed.</li>
<li>More recently however, scientists have begun to see emotions in a more positive light.</li>
<li>Today they are thought of as essential to survival and a major source of personal enrichment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-the-concept">Definition of the concept<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-the-concept" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A complex state of feeling that results in physical and psychological changes that influence thought and behavior.</li>
<li>A complex feeling/state involving conscious experience and internal and overt physical responses that tend to facilitate or inhibit motivated behaviour.</li>
<li>Outward expression of inner feelings.</li>
<li>A motivated state marked by physiological arousal, expressive behaviour, and mental experience.</li>
<li>For example  an angry man:</li>
<li>His heart might pound (physiological arousal)</li>
<li>Feel enraged (mental/cognitive experience)</li>
<li>Grit his teeth (expressive behaviour)</li>
<li>Kosslyn and Rosenberg (2006) propose that emotions have four components:</li>
<li>a positive or negative subjective experience</li>
<li>bodily arousal</li>
<li>the activation of specific mental processes and stored information, and</li>
<li>characteristic behaviour</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>A feeling, or affect, that can involve physiological arousal (a fast heartbeat for example), conscious experience (thinking about being in love with someone, for example), and behavioural expression (a smile or grimace, for example).</p>
<p>Emotion is often associated and considered reciprocally influential with mood, temperament, personality, disposition, and motivation. It also is influenced by hormones and neurotransmitters such as dopamine, noradrenaline, serotonin, oxytocin and cortisol.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="functions-of-emotions">Functions of emotions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#functions-of-emotions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Help guide us to approach some things and withdraw from others</li>
<li>Provide visible cues that help other people know key aspects of our thoughts and desires</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="components-of-emotions">Components of emotions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#components-of-emotions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Cognitive appraisal: provides an evaluation of events and objects</li>
<li>Bodily symptoms: the physiological component of emotional experience</li>
<li>Action tendencies: a motivational component for the preparation and direction of motor responses.</li>
<li>Expression: facial and vocal expression almost always accompanies an emotional state to communicate reaction and intention of actions</li>
<li>Feelings: the subjective experience of emotional state once it has occurred</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="theories-of-emotion">Theories of emotion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#theories-of-emotion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ancient-greece-and-middle-ages">Ancient Greece and Middle Ages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ancient-greece-and-middle-ages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Theories about emotions stretch back to at least as far as the stoics of Ancient Greece and Ancient China.</li>
<li>In the latter it was believed that excess emotion caused damage to <em>qi</em>, which in turn, damages the vital organs.</li>
<li>The four humours theory made popular by Hippocrates contributed to the study of emotion in the same way that it did for medicine</li>
<li>Western philosophy regarded emotion in varying ways. In stoic theories it was seen as a hindrance to reason and therefore a hindrance to virtue.</li>
<li>Aristotle believed that emotions were an essential component to virtue.</li>
<li>In the Aristotelian view all emotions (called passions) corresponded to an appetite or capacity.</li>
<li>During the Middle Ages, the Aristotelian view was adopted and further developed by scholasticism and Thomas Aquinas in particular.</li>
<li>There are also theories in the works of philosophers such as Ren Descartes, Niccol Machiavelli, Baruch Spinoza and David Hume.</li>
<li>In the 19th century emotions were considered adaptive and were studied more frequently from an empiricist psychiatric perspective.</li>
</ul>
