<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="evolutionary-theories">Evolutionary theories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#evolutionary-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-81af617f.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<p>Illustration from Charles Darwin's <em>The Expression of the Emotions in Man and Animals</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="19th-century">19th Century<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#19th-century" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Perspectives on emotions from evolutionary theory were initiated in the late 19th century with Charles Darwin's book <em>The Expression of the Emotions in Man and Animals</em>.</li>
<li>Darwin argued that emotions actually served a purpose for humans, in communication and also in aiding their survival.</li>
<li>Darwin, therefore, argued that emotions evolved via natural selection and therefore have universal cross-cultural counterparts.</li>
<li>Darwin also detailed the virtues of experiencing emotions and the parallel experiences that occur in animals.</li>
<li>This led the way for animal research on emotions and the eventual determination of the neural underpinnings of emotion.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contemporary-theories">Contemporary theories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contemporary-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The major theories of motivation can be grouped into three main categories:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Physiological theories</strong> - suggest that responses within the body are responsible for emotions.</li>
<li><strong>Neurological theories</strong> - propose that activity within the brain leads to emotional responses</li>
<li><strong>Cognitive theories</strong> - these theories argue that thoughts and other mental activity play an essential role in the formation of emotions.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physiological-theories">Physiological theories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiological-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-the-james-lange-theory-of-emotion">a) The James-Lange Theory of Emotion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-the-james-lange-theory-of-emotion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>It was proposed by psychologist William James and physiologist Carl Lange.</li>
<li>It suggests that emotions occur as a result of physiological reactions to events.</li>
</ul>
<p>66</p>
<p>Emotions occur when individuals become aware of a physiological response to an emotion-provoking stimulus.
James believed that you feel emotions after your body reacts.
For example, if a soldier raised his gun the moment he saw you coming, James would say that you would first run, and then feel afraid.
In his 1884 article William James argued that feelings and emotions were secondary to physiological phenomena. In his theory, James proposed that the perception of what he called an "exciting fact" led directly to a physiological response, known as "emotion." To account for different types of emotional experiences, James proposed that stimuli trigger activity in the autonomic nervous system, which in turn produces an emotional experience in the brain. The Danish psychologist Carl Lange also proposed a similar theory at around the same time, and therefore this theory became known as the James-Lange theory. As James wrote, "the perception of bodily changes, as they occur, is the emotion." James further claims that "we feel sad because we cry, angry because we strike, afraid because we tremble, and neither we cry, strike, nor tremble because we are sorry, angry, or fearful, as the case may be."
An example of this theory in action would be as follows: An emotion-evoking stimulus (snake) triggers a pattern of physiological response (increased heart rate, faster breathing, etc.), which is interpreted as a particular emotion (fear).
The issue with the James-Lange theory is that of causation (bodily states causing emotions and being a priori)
The James-Lange theory has remained influential. Its main contribution is the emphasis it places on the embodiment of emotions, especially the argument that changes in the bodily concomitants of emotions can alter their experienced intensity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-the-cannon-bard-theory-of-emotion">b) The Cannon-Bard Theory of Emotion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-the-cannon-bard-theory-of-emotion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This theory suggests that the thalamus simultaneously sends an emotion-provoking stimulus to the brain providing feeling of an emotion, and to the sympathetic nervous system, causing physiological reactions such as sweating, trembling and muscle tension simultaneously.
For example you perceive the threat of being shot, and the results of that perception marshal the body's resources for fleeing or fighting at the same time as they generate an emotion.
Bodily emotions and the experience of emotion arise in tandem/simultaneously.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cognitive-theories">Cognitive theories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cognitive-theories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-schachter-singer-theory-the-two-factor-theory-of-emotion">a) Schachter-Singer Theory/ the two-factor theory of emotion.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-schachter-singer-theory-the-two-factor-theory-of-emotion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This theory suggests that the physiological arousal occurs first, and then the individual must identify the reason behind this arousal in order to experience and label it as an emotion.
Schathter and Jerome Singer (1962) developed a theory of emotion that gave cognition a greater role in emotion.
They proposed that emotion is determined by two main factors:</p>
<ul>
<li>Physiological arousal</li>
<li>Cognitive labeling</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
67
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>They argue that we look to the external world for an explanation of why we are aroused.
We interpret the external cues present and label the emotion.
For example if you feel good after someone has made a pleasant comment to you, you might label the emotion happy. If you feel bad after you have done something wrong, you may label the feeling guilty.
Schachter did agree that physiological reactions played a big role in emotions. He suggested that physiological reactions contributed to emotional experience by facilitating a focused cognitive appraisal of a given physiologically arousing event and that this appraisal was what defined the subjective emotional experience. Emotions were thus a result of two stage process: general physiological arousal, and experience of emotion.
For example, the physiological arousal, heart pounding, in a response to an evoking stimulus, the sight of a lion in the kitchen. The brain then quickly scans the area, to explain the pounding, and notices the lion. Consequently, the brain interprets the pounding heart as being the result of fearing the lion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-richard-lazarus">b) Richard Lazarus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-richard-lazarus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Lazarus (1991) believes cognitive activity is a precondition for emotion.</li>
<li>He says we cognitively appraise ourselves and our social circumstances.</li>
<li>These appraisals which include values, goals, commitments, beliefs, and expectations determine our emotions.</li>
<li>People may feel happy because they have a deep religious commitment, angry because they did not get the raise they expected, or fearful because they expect to fail an exam.</li>
<li>Richard Lazarus argued that emotions must have some cognitive intentionality. The cognitive activity involved in the interpretation of an emotional context may be conscious or unconscious and may or may not take the form of conceptual processing.</li>
<li>Lazarus' theory is very influential; emotion is a disturbance that occurs in the following order:</li>
<li>Cognitive appraisalthe individual assesses the event cognitively, which cues the emotion.</li>
<li>Physiological changesthe cognitive reaction starts biological changes such as increased heart rate or pituitary adrenal response.</li>
<li>Actionthe individual feels the emotion and chooses how to react.</li>
</ul>
<p>For example: Jenny sees a snake.</p>
<ol>
<li>Jenny cognitively assesses the snake in her presence. Cognition allows her to understand it as a danger.</li>
<li>Her brain activates Adrenaline gland which pumps Adrenaline through her blood stream resulting in increased heartbeat.</li>
<li>Jenny screams and runs away.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>69</p>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="behavioral-dimension">Behavioral dimension<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#behavioral-dimension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The facial feedback hypothesis states that facial expressions can influence emotions as well as reflect them.</li>
<li>In this view, facial muscles send signals to the brain, which helps individuals recognize the emotion they are experiencing.</li>
<li>Particular facial expressions alter the flow of blood to particular regions of the brain, thereby evoking particular emotional experiences.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sociocultural-dimension">Sociocultural dimension<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sociocultural-dimension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Paul Ekmans (1980,1996) careful observations reveal that our many faces of emotion do not vary significantly from culture to culture.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="plutchik-s-eight-basic-emotions">Plutchik's Eight Basic Emotions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plutchik-s-eight-basic-emotions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Plutchik's eight basic or primary emotions are Joy, Trust, Fear, Surprise, Sadness, Anticipation, Anger, and Disgust. Each primary emotion has a polar opposite, so that:</p>
<ul>
<li>Joy is the opposite of Sadness</li>
<li>Fear is the opposite of Anger</li>
<li>Anticipation is the opposite of Surprise</li>
<li>Disgust is the opposite of Trust</li>
</ul>
<p>Plutchik created the wheel of emotions in order to illustrate the various relationships among the emotions.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-8dd1ae55.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<p><strong>NB:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>The intensity of emotion decreases as you move outward and increases as you move toward the wheel's center.</li>
<li>The intensity of the emotion is indicated by the color.</li>
<li>The darker the shade, the more intense the emotion.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>For example, anger at its least level of intensity is annoyance. At its highest level of intensity, anger becomes rage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="frustration-and-conflict">FRUSTRATION AND CONFLICT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frustration-and-conflict" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-frustration">Definition of Frustration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-frustration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Frustration is the <strong>blockage of motives by obstacles</strong>. Frustration may also be the delay or interference with the attainment of a goal (heavy traffic jam).</li>
<li>Experienced whenever the <strong>pursuit of a goal is thwarted/impended</strong> or when we are <strong>not able to satisfy a motive</strong>.</li>
<li><strong>Negative emotional state</strong> that occurs when <strong>people are prevented from reaching desired goals</strong>.</li>
<li>Frustration may be threatening or non-threatening. For example, a faulty light bulb may lead to the mild inconvenience of looking for a spare one, while losing money to thieves may be threatening, if one wanted to use the money to pay a hospital bill.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sources-of-frustration">Sources of Frustration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sources-of-frustration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Environmental obstacles</li>
<li>Personal obstacles</li>
<li>Conflicts</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="environmental-obstacles">Environmental Obstacles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#environmental-obstacles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Our physical environment may cause frustration through serious obstacles like earthquakes, tornadoes, famine and floods, among others. Some petty obstacles provided by the environment include traffic jams, tyre burst, and disturbance by barking dogs at night, loud music at night, uneven sidewalks and rain at picnic sites.</li>
<li>The social environment provides obstacles like: rules and regulations that are perceived as being too strict; poor working conditions, racial prejudice and tribalism.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="personal-obstacles">Personal Obstacles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#personal-obstacles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An individual may suffer from frustration if some personal limitation/inadequacy prevents the satisfaction of various motives. These limitations may be real or imagined. Some of the limitations are:</p>
<ul>
<li>lack of skills in games</li>
<li>lack of intellectual potential</li>
<li>lack of economic wealth</li>
<li>excessive fatness or shortness</li>
<li>short sight/long sight, blindness</li>
<li>unusual skin pigmentation (Albinism)</li>
<li>Paralysis or other physical disability yet wants to dance</li>
<li>Unattractiveness</li>
</ul>

<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Shyness</li>
<li>Chronic illness</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="conflicts">Conflicts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conflicts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; Occurs when two or more incompatible motivations or behavioural impulses compete for expression.
&gt; When we face two or more incompatible demands, opportunities, needs or goals. We end up:
&gt; - giving up some of our goals
&gt; - modifying some of them
&gt; - resigning ourselves to not attaining all of our goals</p>
<p>&gt; Our inability to decide on the next course of action in view of the fact that every day, we all come across many situations in which we must make decisions, or select an alternative
&gt; If the state of conflict persists, we often develop frustration e.g. having to decide whether to go to the University for a Degree or getting married; having to choose between two good girlfriends/boyfriends to marry.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-conflicts">Types of conflicts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-conflicts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Psychologists have classified conflicts into the following four categories:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="caa8d9af">a) Approach  Approach Conflict<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#caa8d9af" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>This type of conflict is also known as conflicting attraction.</li>
<li>It results from the existence of two or more equally attractive goals.</li>
<li>These goals are mutually exclusive, that is, we must choose one at the expense of the other.</li>
<li>In this kind of conflict, the period of indecision is dependent on the degree of goal equivalence, in that the longer the period of indecision, the greater the amount of stress and frustration.</li>
<li>Most individuals will move from one goal to the other in an attempt to evaluate them. This process is called vacillation. It is the perceived importance of either goal that helps to decide.</li>
</ul>
<p>In order to escape this conflict, an individual must do the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A decision must be made promptly regarding one of the alternatives, making sure that no feelings of frustration are left behind.</li>
<li>In some cases, it is possible to satisfy one goal at once, leaving the other goal open for satisfaction at a later date.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ae391c9a">b) Approach  Avoidance Conflict<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ae391c9a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>A choice must be made about whether to pursue a single goal that has both attractive and unattractive aspects.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>For example, a journalist may enjoy his work, but may fear the prospect of being injured while covering a story during university students' riot; risking rejection by approaching an attractive person in class; risking your savings by investing in a new business that could fail; wanting to join a high status group but can do so only by endorsing views contrary to one's values.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>An individual needs to make a decision promptly to minimize stress. The decision will involve acceptance of the consequences of one's decision</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2158ac37">c) Avoidance  Avoidance Conflict<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2158ac37" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This conflict occurs when an individual is faced with two unpleasant alternatives.</li>
<li>In this case, it is impossible to avoid one alternative without experiencing the other.</li>
<li>One confronts two undesirable or threatening possibilities, neither of which has any positive attributes i.e. "caught between a rock and a hard place"</li>
<li>e.g. a person with a toothache must choose between the pain of the tooth and the anticipated discomfort of going to the dentist; choosing between finishing a job one intensely dislikes or quitting and being called a failure; a person who has been admitted to the university to pursue a course he does not like- the alternative is to pursue the course of his choice in a less prestigious institution; fear of punishment in school vs. fear of punishment at home etc.</li>
<li>Values and ones character are required to resolve the conflict.</li>
<li>Some people resort to defense mechanisms to postpone making a decision, simply because all the alternatives are unpleasant.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="note">Note:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#note" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Defense mechanisms are the reactions people develop when they wish to avoid making decisions. For example, some people may fantasize that the issue does not exist.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6fafafd1">d) Double Approach  Avoidance Conflict<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6fafafd1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This type of conflict involves two or more alternatives, each of which has both attractive and repulsive qualities- each alternative has both negative and positive qualities</li>
<li>For example, you are offered two jobs; one has good pay but poor hours and dull work; the second has interesting work and excellent hours but low pay; a person may get an offer of a highly paying job abroad but is not allowed to go with his family- the alternative is to stay with his family and continue with the unattractive local job.</li>
<li>An individual must make his choice after careful analysis of the consequences of his decision.</li>
<li>This conflict is difficult to cope with because one is never sure that the correct choice has been made.</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-do-people-react-to-frustration">How do people react to frustration?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-do-people-react-to-frustration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>(Differently due to type of personality, alternatives, level of stress etc)</p>
<ul>
<li>Withdrawal</li>
<li>Hostile feelings directed at the wrong people/scapegoating</li>
<li>Poor grooming</li>
<li>Alcoholism</li>
<li>Workaholics</li>
<li>Excessive smoking</li>
<li>Vulgar expressions</li>
<li>Over religiosity/subservience</li>
<li>May also lead to creativity (new ways of doing things) for those with a strong will.</li>
<li>Use of defense mechanisms</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="defense-mechanisms">Defense mechanisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#defense-mechanisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>We are sometimes forced to use delaying tactics in order to avoid making decisions. These tactics are the defense mechanisms. There are short-term and long-term defense mechanisms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="short-term-defense-mechanisms">Short-term Defense Mechanisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#short-term-defense-mechanisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-restlessness-and-tension">1) Restlessness and Tension<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-restlessness-and-tension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The emotional reactions associated with restlessness and tension includes whimpering, sighing and complaining. These are immediate symptoms of frustration.</li>
<li>Should the amount of stress within the individual escalate, other symptoms exhibited include trembling, clenching of fists, smoking, chewing gum, nail biting and thumb sucking.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-destructiveness">2) Destructiveness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-destructiveness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>They are of two types:</p>
<ul>
<li>Direct aggression: Frustration may lead to destructiveness as well as hostile attacks on all persons and things that are seen causing frustration. Direct aggression involves kicking out, knocking over or breaking and destroying objects.</li>
<li>Indirect aggression: There are two forms of indirect aggression: displaced aggression and scape-goating. Displaced aggression involves aggression against innocent persons or objects rather than the actual cause of frustration. For example, a frustrated employee may harass his family instead of his domineering boss. Scape-goating involves placing blame on an innocent person for one's shortcomings. For example, a student blames his teacher for his low grade.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-apathy-apathy-is-a-state-manifested-through-the-following">3) Apathy: Apathy is a state manifested through the following:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-apathy-apathy-is-a-state-manifested-through-the-following" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Indifferences</li>
<li>Inactivity</li>
<li>Inattentiveness</li>
<li>A general don't care attitude</li>
</ul>
<p>74</p>
<p>NB: Prisoners resort to apathy when they realize that aggression against detention is futile behaviour.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Fantasy:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>This is the tendency to resort to unrealistic solutions.</li>
<li>Fantasy usually involves escapism into the dream world by both adults and children.</li>
<li>In extreme cases, individuals may lose ability to distinguish between reality and the fantasy world.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Stereotype:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>This is the tendency to resort to repetitive, fixed behaviour.</li>
<li>This behaviour is repeated despite its failure to solve problems in previous efforts, for example, thumbing in children.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="6">
<li>Regression:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>It involves a return to primitive modes of behaviour that were present at a younger age. It is often characterized by an attempt to return to a more desirable past stage of develop. Disorganization and collapse of adult behavior and emergence of developmentally lower levels of functioning.</li>
<li>A retreat to an earlier developmental level according to Freud  the persons most fixated stage that demands less mature responses and aspirations.</li>
<li>Return to the use of reaction patterns long since outgrown in the face of adult difficulties yearning for the carefree, sheltered days of childhood e.g. The frustrated adult may resort to temper tantrums of childhood; the bride may run home to mother her at the first sign of trouble; tendency of old people to live more and more in the past and extol its advantages the good old days; in the face of severe stress, an individual may retreat from adult reaction patterns to a less demanding status that involves lowered aspirations and more readily accomplished satisfactions; psychotic adults who show such extreme regression to infantile levels of behavior that they are unable to wash, dress, or feed themselves, or take care of their eliminate needs. In some cases, they curl up in a position similar to that of the foetus in the womb</li>
</ul>
<p>Long Term Defense Mechanisms.</p>
<ol>
<li>Rationalization:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>This involves giving logical reasons or excuses for our behaviour rather than the real/true reason for an action or event / Involves thinking up logical, socially approved reasons for past, present or proposed behavior</li>
<li>For example, a person tells his friend that he didnt get a job because he had no connections; justifying oneself for spending money needed for essentials or lavish entertainment &amp; neglecting work for other pursuits; marriage proposal is turned down by an attractive girl (after all she talks too much, and will probably lose her figure at an early age); a person has very little money- emphasizes that the really important things in life- such as love and friendship are free; mediocre performances e.g. test failure- I am not interested in the course. Besides, I find the teacher extremely dull. I do not care about grades anyway.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Projection:</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>This involves attributing one's own undesirable traits or impulses to other people. Places the blame for his own shortcomings, mistakes and mistakes on others.</p>
<p>Attributes to others his own unacceptable impulses, thoughts and desires (ascribing feelings to someone else that we don't want to acknowledge as our own.)</p>
<ul>
<li>E.g. A student, who feels angry with the teacher, perceives the teacher as being angry with him; the delinquent teenager may blame his problems on a rejecting and non-understanding mother; a lonely divorced woman accuses all men of having only one thing in their minds.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-reaction-formation">3) Reaction formation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-reaction-formation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It involves expressing exaggerated ideas and emotions that are the opposite of the disturbing motive, unconscious impulses or desires/ Repression of dangerous impulses, followed by converting them to their direct opposite</p>
<p>For example, a father may show excessive love to a child he does not like in order to cover up his real feelings; a former prostitute Angel is now a tireless crusader against it; a woman is attracted to a man, but acts coldly towards him concealing hate with a faade of love; a woman who gives birth to an unwanted child becomes extremely overprotective; people who crusade against loose morals, alcohol, gambling, and other real or alleged evils- often such people have a background of earlier difficulties with these problems themselves and their zealous crusade appears to be a means of safeguarding themselves against recurrence of their difficulties.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-dissociation">4) Dissociation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-dissociation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is a mechanism that disrupts the unity of thinking, feeling and acting.</p>
<p>One form of dissociation is compulsive behaviour that is characterized by actions that are not well-coordinated.</p>
<p>A twitching arm may substitute for hitting out in anger.</p>
<p>Dissociation keeps away feelings of guilt by automatically suppressing undesirable acts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-substitution">5) Substitution:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-substitution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It involves substitution of approved goals for unapproved ones. There are two forms of substitution, sublimation and compensation.</p>
<p><strong>Sublimation:</strong> This is a process where socially unacceptable motives are rechanneled into pursuits that the society considers acceptable or admirable. For example, the desire to express aggressions can be channeled to sports or occupation like boxing, and rugby. Sexual energy can be channeled to art and music.</p>
<p><strong>Compensation:</strong></p>
<p> This is a process an individual strives to make up for failure in one activity by making an effort in another activity.</p>
<p> A defense against feelings of inferiority and inadequacy growing out of real or imagined personal defects or weaknesses, as well as out of the individual's inevitable failures and setbacks</p>
<p>For example, a student who is poor in sports may focus his energy into academic work; Theodore Roosevelt waged a valiant fight against ill health to become noted for his physical daring and robustness; physically unattractive person may develop an exceptionally pleasing personality; the boy who feels inferior and unpopular may become the local bully; some people resort to criticism or innuendos in an attempt</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>to cut others down to their own size; engaging in antisocial behavior in an attempt to get attention.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-repression">6) Repression:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-repression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This is a process where stressful things are conveniently forgotten.</li>
<li>Unpleasant memories or disturbing sexual and aggressive impulses are involuntarily removed from consciousness or barred from consciousness.</li>
<li>For example, after failing an exam, you keep forgetting to tell your parents about it; forgetting to go to a dreaded dentist appointment; a soldier who witnesses the horrible death of his friend in combat becomes 'amnesic' with regard to the battle experience; Job forgets a traumatic incident from childhood.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-intellectualization">7) Intellectualization:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-intellectualization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This is an attempt to gain detachment from an emotionally threatening situation.</li>
<li>Threatening thoughts or emotions are kept at arms length by thinking about them logically and rationally</li>
<li>For example, the hurt over a parents death is reduced by saying that he/she lived a full life or died mercifully without pain; failures and disappointments are softened by pointing out that, It could have been worse.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-suppression">8) Suppression:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-suppression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The process of deliberate self-control. It involves keeping impulses or desires in check.</li>
<li>It also involves consciously keeping aside painful memories in order to concentrate on other tasks.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="9-denial">9) Denial:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-denial" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>refusing to accept some aspect of reality because it creates too much anxiety</li>
<li>An attempt to screen  out disagreeable realities by ignoring or refusing to acknowledge them This isnt really happening to me. e.g. a smoker refuses to acknowledge the risks of smoking; refusing to discuss unpleasant topics, fainting when confronted with a traumatic situation, ignoring criticism, becoming pre  occupied with work, hence having no time e.g. to deal with personal problems; one has a drinking problem, but denies that it is a problem.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="10-identification">10) Identification:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#10-identification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Incorporating someone else into our own personality, adopting their behaviour and attitudes.</li>
<li>Taking on the characteristics of someone else so that we can vicariously share in that persons triumphs and overcome feelings of inadequacy.</li>
<li>People who feel inferior may identify themselves with successful causes and people or groups in the hope that they will be perceived as worthwhile.</li>
<li>Enhances feelings of worth and protects the individual against self  devaluation e.g. a parent with unfulfilled career ambitions mare share emotionally in a sons / daughters</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>professional success; adolescence  group identification; adults  identify themselves with their professions, homes, special interest groups and the accomplishments of their children; many employees identify themselves with the power and prestige of the company for which they work.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="11-introductions">11) Introductions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#11-introductions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Involves the acceptance of others values and norms as ones own even when they are contrary to ones previous assumptions e.g. Introjection of Nazi norms in the German concentration camps; an abused child who assumes the abusing parents way of handling stress and thus continuing the cycle of child beating.</li>
<li>Note: Introjection can lead to seriously distorted and maladaptive behavior.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="12-undoing-atonement">12) Undoing (Atonement)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#12-undoing-atonement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A symbolic attempt, often ritualistic or repetitive, to right a wrong or negate some disapproved thought, impulse or act e.g.</li>
<li>Apologizing for wrongs committed against others.</li>
<li>Penance, repentance and undergoing punishment (resolve guilt feelings and make a new beginning).</li>
<li>The unfaithful husband may bring his wife presents.</li>
<li>The unethical businessman may give huge sums of money to charity.</li>
<li>Dishonest businessman  who may be a kind father and a pillar of the church.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="13-displacement">13) Displacement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#13-displacement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Directing an emotion such as hostility or anxiety towards a substitute target e.g.</li>
<li>The meek office clerk who is refused a raise by his domineering boss. Instead of expressing his hostility towards his employer, he goes home and snaps irritably at his wife because supper is a few minutes late.</li>
<li>Sometimes one may turn the hostility inwards (intropunitive) engage in exaggerated self  accusations and recriminations and feel severely guilty (can lead to depression and suicide).</li>
<li>In Nazi Germany, the blame for the countrys ills was attributed to the Jews and Communists/ scapegoating.</li>
<li>Swearing  used as a means of discharging pent  up feelings.</li>
<li>Destructive criticisms and vindictive gossip - disguised methods of expressing hostility.</li>
</ul>
<p>NB: The above defence mechanisms help one to cope with frustration. If there is too much frustration, stress sets in and individuals may become maladjusted or get mental illnesses such as psychosis (madness) and neurosis</p>
